AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: 12. The ATOM’s Effect on the Final Frontier
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: 12-the-atoms-effect-on-the-final-frontier

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/12-the-atoms-effect-on-the-final-frontier.html 
DATE: 06/13/2016 01:10:48 AM
-----
BODY:
<div class="entry-content">
<p style="text-align: center;">&nbsp;</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><em>Astronomy compels the soul to look upwards and leads us from this world to another.</em></p>
<p style="text-align: right;"><em>–&nbsp;</em>Plato</p>
<p><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=07STWBXNoyg&amp;feature=youtu.be">(Video)</a></span>. &nbsp;Ultimately, the endgame of any treatise on future visions invariably marches toward one particular topic.&nbsp; We earlier examined how Third Millennium Economics was creating commercial activity in space for the first time, such as private spaceflight, asteroid mining, and zero-gravity 3D Printing.&nbsp; As sophisticated as this may seem, these are still just stepping stones to how the ATOM links our civilization to space.</p>
<p>When the space race was underway in the 1957-77 period and mankind made seemingly giant leaps, many enthusiasts extrapolated that rate of progress forward and predicted a substantial human presence in space by 2020.&nbsp; That has not happened, for <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://www.howmanypeopleareinspacerightnow.com/">there are presently only an exiguous number of people in space</a></span> (on average about one human out of every billion, at any given time).&nbsp; No humans have been more than a few hundred miles above the Earth’s surface in decades.</p>
<p>Unfortunately, assessments now veer towards the opposite extreme, with proclamations such as “Human civilization peaked in 1969-72 because we haven’t been on the Moon since then!” dominating the discourse.&nbsp;&nbsp; Quite to the contrary, the ATOM has enabled great strides in space exploration.&nbsp; This becomes apparent once one realizes that humans setting foot on an extraterrestrial surface is far from the only measure of progress. &nbsp;This is even despite the fact that landing a man on the Moon would cost far less, as a percentage of US GDP, than it did in 1969-72. &nbsp;The following content is a continuation of my article from 2009, <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2009/05/seti-and-the-singularity.html">SETI and the Singularity</a></span>. &nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p><strong>Space is For the Robots : &nbsp;</strong>For all the popular culture imagery around humans in space, such missions will never be as economical or efficient as sending advanced AI into the heavens.&nbsp; The overwhelming difference in space suitability between humans and AI can scarcely be exaggerated.</p>
<p>An AI does not require air or water, and can survive across a much wider range of temperatures, pressures, gravity, and radiation than a fragile human.&nbsp; An AI can load into a body or bodies (becoming a robot) as needed, or be stored in a tiny volume that is orders of magnitude smaller than what a human crew would require during space travel.&nbsp; The cost divergence begins at the time of launch itself, as it consumes far less fuel to launch a 100 kilogram piece of AI-installed hardware into space than a human-suitable spacecraft that may be 1 million times more massive.&nbsp; This hardware itself continues to shrink for each generation of Moore’s Law, while a ship designed to transport humans does not.&nbsp; Furthermore, if the spacecraft is destroyed in an accident, only the hardware has to be replaced, with the AI software loaded onto it.&nbsp; The tragic deaths and resultant delays associated with failed human missions become a non-issue.</p>
<p>The chasm widens further when one sees how few celestial destinations can host human life.&nbsp; In the entire Solar System there is no world aside from Earth where a human can remotely survive without an elaborate spacesuit, that too for just a short time.&nbsp; By contrast, every single solid world other than Mercury and Venus can host a suitable robotic lander or rover for years.&nbsp; Probes have even landed on comets despite their low gravity.&nbsp; Even with Mercury and Venus, orbital probes with sophisticated AI can operate for decades, and never have to be brought back to Earth.&nbsp; The AI can be endlessly upgraded from Earth via wireless transmission of software updates.&nbsp; Add all of these factors up, and the indisputable advantages in cost, durability, and versatility ensure that most scientific exploration of space will be done with AI housed in relatively small hardware.&nbsp; Each such probe or rover can transmit data back to Earth as well as to other AIs in other locations in space, creating an interplanetary network effect.&nbsp; A few humans may be sent up by their governments for political purposes, and brief recreational space trips for the ultra-wealthy may become a viable business, but that is about the extent of human space travel to occur over the medium term.&nbsp; The uncanny suitability of AI for space leads one to contemplate whether this is some pre-ordained grand design of which we are merely facilitators.</p>
<p>Instead, for the rest of the human population, the celestial will become the virtual.&nbsp; Images and videos beamed back to Earth by the AI will be incorporated into VR experiences, enabling humans to ‘walk’ on the surfaces of Mars, Europa, Callisto, and Titan from their own homes, or even ‘fly’ between worlds faster than the speed of light.&nbsp; More people will be able to experience space with considerable realism, even as real exploration advances without human presence in space.</p>
<p><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p><strong>Exponential Exploration and Discovery : &nbsp;</strong>If humans are to be Earthbound for a long time to come, that does not mean we miss the chance to revel in the growing wave of discoveries.&nbsp; Space exploration, particularly telescope power and data crunching, is being pulled into the ATOM, with the expected rate of exponential progress that entails.&nbsp;</p>
<p>No matter what, the greatest question of all is whether we are alone in the universe, and if we are not, what form has that other life taken.&nbsp; As our technology has advanced, some of the assumptions around this question have begun to shift.&nbsp; This is a vast subject and cannot be done full justice here, but one trend that stands out is the rising power and precision of telescopic methods and their merger with big data and supercomputing.&nbsp; <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/09/telescope_power.html">Back in 2006, I estimated that telescopic power is rising at a compounded rate of 26%/year (the square root of Moore's Law, as pixel count increases as a square of the shrinkage of the side dimension)</a></span>. &nbsp;This has, among other discoveries, resulted in the detection of planets outside of our solar system, known as <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exoplanet">exoplanets</a></span>.</p>
<p>Most stars are too inherently dim to be seen from Earth, unless they are very near (the nearest star, Proxima Centauri, is nonetheless far below the brightness threshold where it might be seen with the naked eye).&nbsp; Since many of the dimmer, cooler stars have planets, and planets only reflect some miniscule fraction of the light they receive from their primary star, a planet within a star system several light years away is vanishingly faint when viewed from Earth.&nbsp; Such planets were impossible to detect until new methods independent of luminosity emerged, such as observing radial velocity and transits of the planet in front of its primary star.&nbsp; Astronomers have continued to refine these methods, and with the technological improvement of their instruments both on the ground and in space, the rate of exoplanet discovery is rising exponentially.</p>
<p><a class="asset-img-link" style="float: left;" href="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20240a4dd0832200d-pi"><img class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20240a4dd0832200d img-responsive" alt="Exoplanet_cumulative" title="Exoplanet_cumulative" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20240a4dd0832200d-320wi" style="margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;" /></a>As recently as 1995, there were hardly any exoplanets identified, but as the chart of annual discoveries shows us, we are now discovering an increasing number of them, in a curve that fits the familiar parabolic trajectory.&nbsp; There are now over 4000 planets confirmed, and the next 4000 will naturally take far less time than the first 4000.&nbsp; Note that newer methods are now generating the most detections (<span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exoplanet">chart from Wikipedia</a></span>).</p>
<p>The majority of early detections were larger, Jupiter-sized planets, and the discovery of Earth-sized planets has only begun more recently.&nbsp; Whether other forms of life require conditions similar to ours remains to be seen, but it is probable that any biological life forms may be just as unsuitable for space as we are.&nbsp; Nonetheless, if a small fraction of worlds with life have reached the threshold of creating their own artificial intelligence, their intelligence is similarly freed of conditional restrictions as ours would be, and then they might be easier to detect or even meet.&nbsp;</p>
<p>However, the paradox of this means that under the accelerating rate of change, it is very hard for a civilization even slightly more advanced than us to avoid detection, due to the much greater presence and detectability it would have.&nbsp; This may explain the <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermi_paradox">Fermi Paradox</a></span>, and increase the chances that we are one of very few advanced civilizations, or at least one of the earliest, and at least in our own galaxy.&nbsp; Over time, the exponentially rising rate of discovery will enable us to narrow down the range of probabilities of extra-terrestrial life and intelligence, and there will be orders of magnitude more candidate planets as soon as the 2020s. &nbsp;For a detailed article about how the ATOM affects SETI and the Drake Equation, and how there is a good chance that we are the most advanced civilization in at least this portion of the galaxy, <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2009/05/seti-and-the-singularity.html">see my 2009 article</a></span>. &nbsp;</p>
<p><a class="asset-img-link" style="float: left;" href="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Known_NEAs.png/1024px-Known_NEAs.png"><img class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20240a4f8e7ed200b img-responsive" alt="image from upload.wikimedia.org" title="image from upload.wikimedia.org" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20240a4f8e7ed200b-320wi" style="margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;" /></a>The second major benefit of telescopic progress is in the <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://www.singularity2050.com/2019/11/atom-award-of-the-month-november-2019.html">detection of asteroid impact threats</a></span>.&nbsp; While there was a real risk of a surprise impact before 2000 (recall the films made in the 1990s about exactly this sort of disaster) the expansion of telescopic power has since identified almost all risky Near Earth Objects (NEOs) to date, revealing which are an impact risk at what point in time.&nbsp; Almost all objects large enough to be a problem have been mapped, and none are expected to strike the Earth in the next 20 years, by which time our technologies for detection, deflection, and even capturing of asteroids will be far more advanced.&nbsp; Notice the same exponential chart appear here yet again, further proving that telescopic power is yet another crucial technology that went from low-tech to high-tech once the ATOM got to it.&nbsp; Just like 'Peak Oil' before it, the ATOM has made a previous, seemingly insurmountable problem all but vanish through sheer force of technological progress.&nbsp; Even better, the ATOM will convert this into a business opportunity, as these asteroids contain billions of tons of useful metals and hydrocarbons.&nbsp; There are a number of 'precious' metals on Earth that are in fact far more abundant in asteroids, and the supply of these metals could rise dramatically, sending the price lower and contributing further to ATOM-derived deflation.&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</p>
<p>As we can see, the majority of future space activity does not involve manned space missions.&nbsp; In contrast, with the ATOM converging discovery technologies into a rapid rate of improvement, astronomical research has become an information technology.&nbsp; This dichotomy does not fit into old assumptions about how space might be explored, but there has never been a better time to be a space enthusiast, whether scientific, industrial, or philosophical.&nbsp; This is a statement that can only become increasingly true each passing year.</p>
<p>Continue to :&nbsp;<span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://atom.singularity2050.com/13-conclusion.html">13. Conclusion</a></span></p>
</div>

COMMENT:
AUTHOR: Geoman
EMAIL: paul.dworian@aecom.com
IP: 209.165.164.244
URL: 
DATE: 08/08/2016 10:07:30 AM
I think this section could be beefed up.

Ultimately the cost for anything will boil down to three things 1) the cost of labor to make it 2) the cost of the materials to make it 3) the cost of the energy to make it.

With AI and robotics, cost #1 goes down to a very low number indeed.  With nanotech and 3d printing, the cost for #2 is also falling.  And with solar power, the cost for #3 is falling.  As each of these costs fall, they feed into declines in the other sectors, for example, robotics makes manufacturing solar cells cheaper, which makes energy cheaper.  

In space, there is the potential for an order of magnitude drop in energy and materials costs.  Solar energy is plentiful, and twice as strong as on the surface of the Earth.  Many rare elements, such as gold and platinum, occur in great abundance. What has always prevented exploitation of these resources is the lack of AI - putting people in space to control the process was always a very expensive proposition.  Having a robot do it...well, it pretty much removes the last obstacle.  

There are now two companies that plan to mine asteroids.  Looking over their plans...it could work.  seriously, for a reasonable and finite investment, they might just be able to conduct mining.

what if gold or palladium became available at a few buck a kg?  Wouldn't that spur all sorts of technological advancements that have be stifled due to the price of these materials?

AI and space has the potential to cut the costs of elements and energy to fractions of what we see right now.  What if the U.S. simply mined enough gold in place to put the U.S. back on the gold standard?  Oh yeah, and pay off the national debt.  In gold.  The extreme economic changes that could result from such a situation are hard to imagine.

AUTHOR: agimarc

The most valuable substance to be mined off planet will be water ice.  Given any sort of a power source, water ice becomes propellant, atmosphere, growing medium and radiation shielding.  The cool thing is that the farther from the sun you get, the more you find.  Even Mars gets wetter the more you look at it, though the water is generally tied up as water ice.  The solar system even delivers substantial icy bodies into the inner solar system via the earth orbit crossing asteroids and comets.  Fully half of the earth crossing asteroids are thought to be inactive comets with substantial percentages of water and other ices.  Some of them are very close to earth orbit in terms of delta-v for a trip, though the trip times will be long.  Enabling technology for this would be reactor(s) of some sort for power / propulsion (think the old Nerva as an example).  I came across a guy who worked with Gene Shoemaker who worked out how low temperature nuclear powered steam rockets will open the solar system pretty quickly.  URLs available upon request.   Cheers -

We will colonize the moon and mars soon.  Within the next decade.

Magellan's crew circumnavigated the globe 1522. It took 4 years. The second circumnavigation was....Drake in 1580.  55 years later. It also took 4 years. The third circumnavigation was 1584.  Four years after the second. 4 years to complete.  The forth was 1588.  Four years.  But completed one year faster. The fifth was One year later.  The sixth was in 1590.  One year later.  By that point we stopped counting individual circumnavigations because they became so common.

Now the international space station circles the globe every 92 minutes.

Our last landing on the moon was 1972.  48 years ago.  Anyone think we won't land on the moon again in the next 7 years?  And that once we do, we won't land again every few years?

SpaceX is saying their entire star ship rocket program, with a flight around the moon and/or landing, will cost around $10 billion to develop and first launch.  Apollo's cost was...$152 billion in today's dollars. 1/15th the cost.

The cost to put a man on the moon per person was $12.6 billion.  And the deaths of three astronaut, and near deaths of three more.  Companies are now saying they can land people on the moon for <$750 million per person. Again, right around 1/15th the cost.

In 2019 dollars, U.S. GDP was $5.4 trillion in 1972.  Today it is $20 trillion. We have 4 times more money to pay 1/15th the cost for a lunar landing. And the economy is still growing, and the lunar landing costs are still declining.

We will land on the moon and mars soon, because the costs to do so will become trivial expenditures.

Geoman,

AI, hence, is still far cheaper than a human in space, for any exploratory or research purpose.  The trendline you describe might in fact continue via unmanned, rather than manned, missions.  

Given the small size of AI, thousands of AIs in small, smartphone-sized hardware units can be launched in all directions (as against the one-ton crafts like Voyager I &2, etc.).  

Humans in space for recreation is still the only market.  But I still say that a time when thousands of people are at least as far away as the Moon, and remain so on a long-term basis, is very, very far away, if it ever happens at all.

<a href="https://www.space.com/minor-planets-beyond-neptune-dark-energy-survey.html">https://www.space.com/minor-planets-beyond-neptune-dark-energy-survey.html</a>

139 new objects discovered all at once in orbit beyond Neptune. The researchers started out with 7 billion dots, which they whittled down to 22 million "transients" after ruling out objects that appeared in roughly the same spot on multiple nights. Those 22 million were further culled to 400 planetary candidates.  Of those 319 were small trans neptunium objects.  139 were new to science.

It's not just robotics.  Increasingly we don't even need to send robots anywhere - just crunch huge masses of data to get the answer.

TITLE: 11. Implementation of Third Millennium Economics for Individuals
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: 11-implementation-of-the-atom-age-for-individuals

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/11-implementation-of-the-atom-age-for-individuals.html 
DATE: 06/13/2016 01:08:32 AM
-----
BODY:
<div class="entry-content">
<p style="text-align: center;">&nbsp;</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><em>&nbsp;</em><em>Live as if you were to die tomorrow.&nbsp; Learn as if you were to live forever.</em></p>
<p style="text-align: right;"><em>–</em><em>&nbsp;</em>Mahatma Gandhi</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><em>Ability will never catch up with the demand for it.</em></p>
<p style="text-align: right;"><em>–&nbsp;</em>Confucius</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=m635uQdFax0&amp;feature=youtu.be">(Video)</a></span>. &nbsp;Building upon the meme presented in the previous section on how the future is not evenly distributed, it is now time to take steps to get more of the future distributed in your favor.&nbsp; As an individual, there are a myriad of resources and capabilities at your fingertips, that can make you healthy, wealthy, and wise with a speed and efficiency that was previously impossible.<strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><strong>How to Think Like Part of the ATOM : &nbsp;</strong>You are not merely a hapless creature being swept along by the ATOM, you are a part of it.&nbsp; Whether a person is interested in the subject matter discussed here or not, the ATOM is interested in them.&nbsp; Every aspect of life is being woven into the ATOM as existing hierarchies and power structures are being toppled and re-arranged at accelerating speeds.&nbsp; Therein lies tremendous opportunity, and thus opportunity cost for those who fail to become sufficiently astute.&nbsp; Just consider a few of the examples that come to mind :</p>
<ul>
<li>Never before has it been easier to research a career, or to contact someone who is in a position you wish to attain in the future. A skilled and determined user of LinkedIn can replace much of the networking utility of an MBA degree from all but the top few institutions, saving considerable time and expense.&nbsp; The ability to keep track of the career progression of dozens of peers, and identify common elements of success among them, is often underestimated.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>An amateur filmmaker now has access to high-definition cameras, editing software, terabytes of storage, and sound synthesizers that would have been prohibitively expensive just a decade ago. A film project can be financed through a crowdfunding site, the work and reputation of potential collaborators can be researched easily through their web presence, and trailers can be marketed through YouTube.&nbsp; Such a project has at least six components that did not exist at the start of the century.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>Before Yelp and other review aggregators, there was considerable uncertainly when patronizing a new restaurant. Now, not only are user reviews easy to find, but pictures of many menu items are posted on Yelp itself.&nbsp; This information arms the diner with much more awareness of the menu, and enables the restaurant to avoid being castigated due to a misinformed order by the diner.&nbsp; The same applies to checking the reviews of a new film or theater production beforehand, greatly reducing the chance of a negative surprise after the sunk cost of tickets.&nbsp; Dentists, auto mechanics, photographers, hairdressers, etc. can be similarly vetted from a computer screen.&nbsp; This new convenience to the consumer experience is not recorded favorably in GDP calculations, even though millions of instances of dissatisfaction and lost time have been avoided.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>Automobile commuters with good jobs but lengthy commutes have joined Uber-type platforms to take a rider along with them on <em>the commute they have to undertake anyway</em>. The driver earns an extra $200-$400/week (against which an appropriate portion of car and smartphone costs can be applied as deductions) with no incremental input time or cost.&nbsp; Meanwhile, other commuters enjoy having one less car on the road for each such dynamically generated carpooling pair.&nbsp; The key is that a dead commute is now monetized even by corporate-class people, increasing passengers per car and reducing traffic congestion, while replacing dedicated taxicabs.&nbsp; For the macroeconomy, it also creates new VM where none existed before.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>A trifecta of new technologies has enabled small manufacturers to experience a phoenix-like resurgence in the US. Fracking has toppled the price of US natural gas to a sixth of what it was in 2007, bestowing any gas-intensive manufacturer with a major cost advantage over non-US rivals.&nbsp; Low-cost 3D Printing has lowered scale as a barrier to entry for many types of manufacturing and prototyping.&nbsp; Manufacturing robots can perform many tasks better than human workers, 24/7/365, at a cost as low as $1/hour, while being continuously augmented with new software updates.&nbsp; The rise of the solo advanced manufacturer is now upon us, with individuals operating out of a small space producing and selling millions of dollars of high-margin goods.</li>
</ul>
<p>Have you done enough aggregate Internet searching and forum commenting to capture all the low-hanging fruits available to you for your personal advancement and risk management?&nbsp; If you answer that question in the affirmative, I am here to tell you that you have not come even close to realizing what is possible.&nbsp; Even heavily committed people barely access 10% of the information that could greatly improve their careers, finances, health, and relationships, and I don’t think there is anyone in the world, no matter how successful, who has implemented more than 50% of what is available to them.&nbsp; I myself am nowhere near this level.</p>
<p>Recall the earlier point about how it is now possible to research in minutes what used to take half a day in the public library (that too if you were fortunate enough to be in a country that even had public libraries at the time).&nbsp;&nbsp; Add to that the ability to get your questions answered in forums like Yahoo Answers, Quora, etc.&nbsp; Then combine that with connections between different memes, factoids, and tangents that would not have been visible in the glacial pace of information accrual and exchange before Internet search and forums.&nbsp; Integrating all this, you can see how your ability to access and implement valuable information can take a great leap forward, and how almost everyone can participate in the creation of knowledge.</p>
<p><a class="asset-img-link" href="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20240a4fdfc66200d-pi"><img class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20240a4fdfc66200d img-responsive" alt="ATOM small" title="ATOM small" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20240a4fdfc66200d-320wi" style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" /></a></p>
<p><strong>Case Studies of Personal Third Millennium Economics : &nbsp;</strong>Sometimes, a story can better illustrate the ways a person savvy to the disruptive and augmentative nature of new technologies can rapidly upgrade one or more aspects of their life.&nbsp; There is more fluidity and mobility across classes and strata than was possible before, and the ATOM is now the land of opportunity.&nbsp; The following are four examples of how someone might adapt and thrive within the new realities of Third Millennium Economics.</p>
<p>1) Lisa wanted to become an exceptionally good amateur chef, but back in 1998, she found the available instructional materials to be limited and uninspiring.&nbsp; Every cooking show on TV required the viewer to be interested in the item being presented in that specific episode, with no way for the viewer to search for their own preferences.&nbsp; Cookbooks were not a good solution either, for each ingredient listed in a recipe required that item to be purchased in a larger quantity, leaving remainder quantities of each item in the refrigerator and pantry.&nbsp; Lisa wondered for years <em>why none of these cookbooks had a matrix in the back of the book, linking ingredients across recipes, to make the shopping process for the layperson more efficient and less wasteful.&nbsp; </em>Lisa found this oversight among the sum total of published cookbooks to be quite ridiculous.&nbsp; She knew that there was major overlap in the basics of cooking, but this was not easy for an amateur to discover without taking an expensive cooking class.</p>
<p>By around 2004, however, something began to change.&nbsp; Lisa found that many of the premier French, Italian, and Indian chefs were posting knowledge online.&nbsp; The common theme among them began to emerge.&nbsp; Great chefs think not in terms of compliance to a fixed recipe, but rather see what ingredients are available, and create a production from them.&nbsp; Cooking has to be bottom-up, not top-down.&nbsp; The ‘eureka’ moment for Lisa was that she could simply type ingredients into Google, and recipes that utilize those ingredients would come up.&nbsp; This was vastly more efficient than a cookbook, and allowed Lisa to get past the learning bottleneck holding her and others back.&nbsp; She could also now buy certain perishables without worrying about exactly what she will make from them as the clock ticks.&nbsp; The arrival of YouTube was another godsend, where, unlike a regularly scheduled cooking show, the user can merely search for whatever recipe she wants.&nbsp; A video is far easier to emulate than a text recipe, and further expanded upon the list of perishable ingredients she can now purchase in a shopping trip.</p>
<p>After years of stagnation, Lisa’s skills improved rapidly after these revelations, and she even posted some of her own cooking videos to YouTube.&nbsp; Comment feedback from like-minded viewers led to additional improvements.&nbsp; Lisa eventually reached a level of expertise where she was able to produce cuisine that met or exceeded what was available at fine restaurants, with her YouTube channel accruing over 1,000,000 subscribers, elevating her hobby to a full-time, commute-free career.</p>
<p>2) Fred always had a keen financial mind, and wanted to do better with generating a return in his IRA and his brokerage account, for he knew that this is just as important as his paycheck from his day job.&nbsp; He did not believe in individual stocks, for he knew he was unlikely to ever close the information disadvantage he had relative to institutions and those very close to the companies.&nbsp; He also found mutual funds to be uninspiring due to their high fees, and the inability to short them or write options on them.</p>
<p>The advent of Yahoo Finance in the late 1990s made a wealth of information available for free, but most of the information was still about individual stocks, which was not Fred’s target.&nbsp; In the process of mining Yahoo Finance, he found a great deal of information on options, as well as daily quotes that were previously unavailable so freely.&nbsp; It was exceedingly difficult for a layperson to research, let alone trade options before the late 1990s, with truly abundant information only appearing around 2005 or so.&nbsp; The discovery process made Fred knowledgeable about options, and eventually futures.&nbsp; As computing and data transmission became cheaper, brokerage firms were able to lower their trading commissions.&nbsp; Fred began to create algorithms to generate returns strictly from options and futures of broadly traded commodities, such as oil, gold, natural gas, and ^vix volatility.&nbsp; Despite some early setbacks, he eventually was able to generate 30-50% annual compounded returns from his algorithms, built around the principle of capturing the various time decays (option decay, futures contango, leverage decay) inherent to those instruments.</p>
<p>As these returns became routine for Fred, his day job became optional, and he gave some thought to managing client money full-time and establishing a hedge fund.&nbsp; Fortunately, in the Internet age, it was easy for him to locate attorneys, auditors, and other service providers.&nbsp; By shopping around, he found that the Internet had increased competition amongst these providers, and thus the fixed costs associated with operating a hedge fund have fallen from about $500,000/year to just $30,000/year, greatly reducing the minimum assets needed for the hedge fund to be viable.&nbsp; Fred’s hedge fund became sustainable with just $10 Million in assets, a threshold unheard of in the past, and he joined the new layer of hedge funds with under $20 Million in assets under management returning over 30%/year.&nbsp; &nbsp;The ability to run his fund from anywhere enabled him to relocate to a preferred destination.</p>
<p>This story is not to say that the percentage of people who become good at generating returns has increased, but rather accessibility is now far more democratic, permitting talented people from outside the establishment to make use of skills that may have gone to waste in the past.&nbsp; On this front, we are still only at the beginning.</p>
<p>3) James is an accountant, and has been employed at a large multinational corporation for over 15 years.&nbsp; As a top performer, he enjoys higher job security than his peers, but is nonetheless apprehensive about the speed at which his colleagues are vanishing and being subsumed by AI.&nbsp; After reading a number of frightening articles in the media, James was deeply worried about how long he could stay ahead of the machines.</p>
<p>Then, one fine day, James came across this publication, and began to see AI in a different light.&nbsp; It dawned on him that what he was observing was not the utter disappearance of accounting jobs into thin air, but that an accounting department with ten accountants at a payroll cost of $2M/yr could now generate the same services for a mere $100,000/yr (and falling) in AI costs and a sole human manager.&nbsp; James started to ponder the implications of starting his own accounting firm, where the work that could bring in $2M/yr in fees could be performed just by him and this new AI capability.&nbsp; His deep knowledge of accounting and reputation in his field meant he could accommodate a large portion of his former employer’s accounting work into his own private practice, on top of acquiring additional clients.&nbsp; One man and his AI was now doing the work of ten, and could earn the income formerly earned by those ten.&nbsp; Until now, most solo practitioner accountants only handled smaller individual clients, and it was unheard of that a single-person firm could presume to undertake the enormity of work generated by an entire corporate department.&nbsp; James was one of the revolutionaries changing this, even though he never thought he would become an entrepreneur.</p>
<p>As happens whenever a new business model becomes highly profitable, James’ success attracted competitors and pricing pressures began to manifest.&nbsp; At the same time, this competition created many additional jobs in sales, marketing, and support.&nbsp; Next, the market itself grew from the entry of smaller firms that could now avail themselves of elite accounting services previously beyond their reach.&nbsp; The ecosystem began to mature, but James continued to earn several times more than he did as a corporate employee, simply by continuing to refine the implementation of AI in his practice.</p>
<p>Lastly, what of the other accountants who saw their positions eliminated, and are not as talented as James?&nbsp; Well, some were able to get jobs in accounting working alongside AI, while others had to transition to different careers.&nbsp; This illustrates why the cushioning effect of the universal minimum stipend supplied by central bank monetary expansion is so essential, as it ensures that everyone gets some return from pervasive and accelerating forces of technological disruption while conducting their own transitions.</p>
<p>4) The legendary 'Delta Team' is a team of three video game creators who had produced a sequence of extremely successful titles.&nbsp; The team was widely acclaimed in their field, and they were able to do almost every aspect of game production for a top-flight game by themselves.&nbsp; But in the past, they were dependent on working within large game companies for the brand, corporate-class resources, and distribution channel.&nbsp;</p>
<p>As the Delta Team's successes accumulated, even the brand of the large company that housed them became secondary to their own, but the distribution channel kept them chained.&nbsp; Video games in 2006 sold as boxed products, and while one could buy the box online, most sales were still through brick and mortar stores.&nbsp; The boxed product in the form of CDs or DVDs did not have a large fixed cost per unit (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Video_game_crash_of_1983">unlike the cartridges that caused an infamous inventory bust in 1983</a>), but still required capital outlay, production time, packaging, and shipping.&nbsp; After that, the retailer margin consumed 30% of the retail price, even stocking a retail channel had onerous requirements of inventory.&nbsp; This had to be done again and again for each country, often with translation requirements for retail packaging, leading to complicated supply/demand mismatches that were tedious to rectify.&nbsp; This led to each boxed game selling for a retail price of $50.&nbsp; Hence, over 20,000 boxed copies had to be produced for a rudimentary launch, and that too only for the US, UK, and Australia.&nbsp; Unsold units carried a risk of an expensive write-down.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<p>But as the ATOM progressed, the convergence of broadband, anti-piracy technologies, price declines of advanced tools, and more, it became possible for the Delta Team to produce games entirely by themselves.&nbsp; Their YouTube livestreams of gameplay during development created a direct connection between the developers and their customers, which enabled feedback to be incorporated into the game itself.&nbsp; Furthermore, they were now able to sell games exclusively through a website via unique downloads, thereby removing dependency on the big corporate umbrella and large retailers that require upfront inventory, as well as country-by-country permits and retail localization.&nbsp; Suddenly, forecasting sales was no longer an important part of the equation, as long as revenue met a minimum threshold for a certain game to be justified on the production roadmap.&nbsp;</p>
<p>The Delta Team could now operate as their own independent company for the first time ever.&nbsp; In this age, they could sell games for $30 instead of the previous $50, while earning about the same per unit sale.&nbsp; Customers in countries that would have been too cumbersome to stock with retail inventory in 2006 were instantly available.&nbsp; Best of all, the situation of boxed games needing to be destocked from brick and mortar retail because a certain retail chain required at least 300 units/week in sales no longer existed, as residual sales for successful games continued for years after this point, which could be serviced by the download purchase model.&nbsp; The Delta Team collectively agreed to relocate to a tax haven and produce games on their own.&nbsp; They churned out game after game that sold 100,000 units or more, or $3 Million in revenue each.&nbsp; Hence, all three members of the Delta Team achieved a great combination of success and independence.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<p><a class="asset-img-link" href="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20240a4fdfc82200d-pi"><img class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20240a4fdfc82200d img-responsive" alt="ATOM small" title="ATOM small" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20240a4fdfc82200d-320wi" style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" /></a></p>
<p>These are just four examples of how someone might view the advent of accelerating technological disruption as an endless stream of opportunity, no matter what field someone works in.&nbsp; I have detailed maps for how specific professions, such as engineering, marketing, product management, investment banking, management consulting, medicine, and law could each supercharge their careers with greater Third Millennium Economics awareness.&nbsp; The savvy and observant individual has a galaxy of avenues from which to choose from, ensuring that more of the future is distributed towards them.&nbsp; Everyone is surrounded by dozens of such avenues to pursue, no matter what your technical expertise, age, or station in life.</p>
<p>Continue to :&nbsp;<span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://atom.singularity2050.com/12-the-atoms-effect-on-the-final-frontier.html">12. The ATOM’s Effect on the Final Frontier</a></span></p>

Scott,

That is good.  You are thinking the right way.  To generate an income that was not possible before is precisely the right track to be on.  

To speed up income gains, you can take some copyediting work and have one of those offshore service providers do it for cheap.  If that saves you time and increases your book output, that is a productivity increase.

AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: 10. Implementation of Third Millennium Economics for Nations
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: 10-implementation-of-the-atom-age-for-nations

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/10-implementation-of-the-atom-age-for-nations.html 
DATE: 06/13/2016 01:07:02 AM
-----
BODY:
<div class="entry-content">
<p style="text-align: center;">&nbsp;</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><em>If a man is proud of his wealth, he should not be praised until it is known how he employs it.</em></p>
<p style="text-align: right;"><em>–&nbsp;</em>Socrates</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=PZXQ1Feu-WI&amp;feature=youtu.be">(Video)</a></span>. &nbsp;Given how recently the world economy has entered this situation of accelerating technological deflation, only the most prosperous and technology-dense countries currently have the ability to generate central bank money in a perpetual and rising stream, and in turn divert it to fund their government spending.&nbsp; Sometimes, a country is closer or further from this threshold than countries of similar prosperity.</p>
<p>While the transformation is a continuum, in general, a nation has to cross a threshold where technological products are approximately 3% of its GDP, which the US has recently surpassed.&nbsp; At the 3% threshold, there is a sufficient buffer against inflation upticks in the early incubatory period, where critics still have followers.&nbsp; For example, the nations of Northwestern Europe could embark on this restructuring right away, but the nations of Southern and Eastern Europe are not quite there yet.&nbsp; The EU cannot easily blend the varying ATOM levels of different countries into one and expect ECB monetary expansion to diffuse evenly.&nbsp; Similarly, while China as of 2020 has a similar prosperity as Latin America, the technological depth of China’s economy makes China fewer years away from this transformation than Mexico and Brazil are.</p>
<p>There was a time when vast reserves of natural resources (such as oil) were seen as the most fortuitous stroke of luck for a nation to have.&nbsp; In this age, the tables are turned, where having significant ATOM density is an even more profitable resource, and unlike oil, is self-reinforcing and ever-expanding.&nbsp; Ironically, the countries that were the most deprived of natural resources (such as Japan and South Korea) had to rely heavily on technology to further their economies, which has now made them among the most ready for the marriage of the ATOM with central bank monetary creation.</p>
<p>While the US has reached a point where it can implement this only at this stage at $65,000 per capita GDP, China could implement this upon reaching a mere $20,000 per capita in the mid-2020s.&nbsp; This is simply due to the percentage of Chinese GDP that will be composed of ATOM-derived deflationary products approaching 3%, despite being merely at a similar level of prosperity as the US was in the 1970s.&nbsp; Even impoverished, low-tech India can implement this by the 2030s, despite still being much poorer at that point than the US is in 2020, due to the same 3% threshold.</p>
<p>Each country has to do an ATOM assessment to determine if their central bank can generate money without generating inflation, and if not, how many years away they might be from such a capability.&nbsp; I have detailed data and algorithms that can help each country estimate where it is on the progression towards the Third Millennium Economics threshold, but for the purposes of this publication, I will discuss a few select countries and their specific characteristics, starting with the United States.</p>
<p><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p><strong>How the US Can Inaugurate Third Millennium Economics </strong>:&nbsp; I repeat that there is virtually no chance that these topics will be researched, the ideological barriers overcome, the Federal Reserve’s powers expanded, and an ATOM-ready DUES program implemented before it is done by a number of smaller countries first.&nbsp; I am also relatively certain that the US will not be the first, second, or even fourth country to implement such a revamp of its monetary and fiscal paradigms (more on those other countries later).&nbsp; But if the US were to hypothetically start this program by 2020, one attractive avenue (as an alternative to starting with the flat $5000/year to all US citizen adults) to ease into it is through that venerable old program, Social Security.</p>
<p>As of 2019, Social Security (SS) collected about $1.2 Trillion in taxes, and distributed roughly the same $1.2 Trillion sum out to recipients.&nbsp; The tax is 12.4% of the employee’s income, up to $137,700 of income, after which there is no tax.&nbsp; While there is an illusion that this 12.4% is split between the employee and employer, this is obviously money that would otherwise land in the pocket of either if not for the tax, and is certainly a contributor to angst about the ‘lack of wage growth’.&nbsp; The SS payout formula calculates payments with some correlation to how much a worker paid into the system, but the correlation is not exact.&nbsp; There are other complicated calculations for spouses of workers, immigrants who were in America for a period but never became US citizens, and so on, all of which are often gamed and exploited.&nbsp; There is the additional cost incurred by the fact that millions of households would not need to file tax returns at all if not for SS, which adds volume to the already-bursting IRS processing system.</p>
<p>There is a great deal of apprehension about the future of the SS program, since US demographics are far less favorable than they were in the first 70 years of SS.&nbsp; The age at which a person can receive benefits was set when US life expectancy was itself around 65, and now, the powerful senior lobby prevents the government from indexing the eligibility age to rising life expectancy.&nbsp; This means that the average duration of receivership has increased from 2-3 years to 12-15 years, or roughly six times longer, even though the duration of working-age payments into the system has not increased.&nbsp; The ratio of intake to payouts has thus been very adversely affected by what should be celebrated – rising life expectancy.&nbsp; This in turn leads to a rather peculiar and morbid opposition to rising lifespans in some quarters, as if the fear of updating a government program from another era is more important than the single most comprehensive indicator of societal well-being.</p>
<p>Instead, since we know that the central banks of the world will soon have to permanently generate well above $4 Trillion/year, it would be appropriate to directly fund all SS obligations with that very monetary influx, making good use of this established distribution channel.&nbsp; Simultaneously, the regressive SS tax can be the first income tax to be eliminated.&nbsp; The recipients see no change in their payment amount, but gain the eventual benefit of their SS money now being exempt from income tax (unlike today) due to the commencement of the income tax phase-out.&nbsp; Note that the monthly increases in the ATOM stipend are far more granular than the annual SS increases, to facilitate dynamic responses to inflation changes.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, working-age people and their employers immediately get more money in their hands to the tune of 12.4% of their paychecks up to the first $137,700, which creates a huge surge in consumer spending and job creation.&nbsp; Debt servicing becomes easier for individuals, and since all other types of income tax are still in effect, those tax collections rise as a ripple effect.&nbsp; The US enjoys a win-win all around, all because the Federal Reserve has to produce that much money anyway to offset technological deflation.</p>
<p><a class="asset-img-link" style="float: left;" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e201b7c86ce597970b-pi"><img class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e201b7c86ce597970b img-responsive" style="margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;" title="Old and New" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e201b7c86ce597970b-500wi" alt="Old and New" /></a>After the inaugural year of funding a government spending program with Federal Reserve QE proves that neither the inflation rate nor fiscal budget was destroyed in the process, it is time to increase this disbursement by the annual 16-24% to match the higher degree of technological penetration in the economy.&nbsp; This should be done by merely extending the payout down the age brackets to people younger than the SS recipients, one age cohort at a time.&nbsp; By 2020, every US citizen over the age of 18 is receiving their payout, while the Federal Reserve is now dispensing over $2.3 Trillion per year ($10,000+/year/person), with broad recognition that Federal Reserve QE has to permanently rise at 16-24%/year.&nbsp; Inflation is no longer a serious concern, since the expansion is within the aegis of Third Millennium Economics.</p>
<p>As the DUES is extended to all US citizens, other government wealth transfer programs should be steadily and proportionally phased out.&nbsp; The order in which this is done has to be decided by policy researchers, but over the 3-5 years that it takes to extend the stipend all the way down to age 18, every Federal payment program from Medicare to the Pell Grant to food stamps should commence the retirement process.&nbsp; This achieves the goal of replacing the 75% of US government spending comprised of payments to individuals with the vastly more efficient and fair DUES.&nbsp; The stipend will rapidly become large enough where it can allow anyone to purchase private health insurance of varying coverage.&nbsp; This retires the system of the government paying healthcare providers directly, thereby distorting prices, reducing efficiency, and throttling innovation.</p>
<p>Simultaneously, the rest of the income tax code can follow the SS tax into the pages of history.&nbsp; This is best achieved by sequentially eliminating each category of tax one by one, based on how similar to a payment program it already is.&nbsp; After the SS tax, perhaps the Medicare tax can be next, followed by the AMT, the capital gains tax, the corporate income tax, and finally, the ordinary/individual income tax.&nbsp; Note that as each tax is eliminated, the collections from each remaining type of tax balloon from the economic stimulus created by the absence of the previous tax.&nbsp; This cushion should not give pause to the plan of systematically phasing out every one of these taxes, since humans are already handicapped relative to the untaxable and borderless output of AI.</p>
<p>State and local governments do not have their own central banks and cannot run budget deficits, so will have to continue to fund themselves with existing methods of taxation, which vary greatly by location.&nbsp; Some states, like Texas, Florida, and Nevada have no income tax, while others like California have top bracket rates as high as 13.3%.&nbsp; Some cities have no income tax, and property taxes also vary greatly.&nbsp; High-tax states might see the vacancy created by the absence of Federal income tax as an excuse to greatly increase state income tax, but this would be a blunder, as the absence of Federal income tax will greatly widen the spread between states with unequal business friendliness.&nbsp; Furthermore, states with income tax rely on the Federal 1040 for a verification of taxable income, but once the Federal 1040 is gone, states will have to do their own auditing and verification.&nbsp; The competition between states and cities will become more direct, which, in a way, was the original intention of the Founding Fathers over two centuries ago.</p>
<p>The remaining 25% (and shrinking) of US Federal spending not in the form of payments to individuals can also be funded with money originated by the Federal Reserve.&nbsp; Just as with state and local governments, the real test with this component of government spending is whether the voters and officials can keep these expenditures contained, or succumb to the urge to overspend.&nbsp; Maintenance of the Third Millennium Economy, <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://atom.singularity2050.com/7-government-policies-must-adapt-and-quickly.html">as described earlier</a></span>, will be a key determinant of competitiveness between nations, with societies that fail to maintain this resource swiftly incurring the penalty of seeing others surpass them.</p>
<p>Lest there be any confusion about the speed at which such a transition could be managed, I emphasize that the full expenditures of the US Federal government <em>cannot</em> hypothetically be funded as soon as 2020 by central bank action alone, as the ATOM is just not broad and deep enough yet to produce enough deflation.&nbsp; Rather, the transition has to be structured to intercept the ATOM at a point a decade away, and staged accordingly.&nbsp; By around 2025, unless another country moves first to capture the entire world's ATOM dividend for itself, the previous calculations predict that the ATOM will be advanced enough to metabolize a level of perma-QE where every US citizen over age 18 gets a stipend that by then has grown to $12,000/year in current dollars and keeps rising each year, pushing ‘Real’ GDP growth rates higher despite low inflation.&nbsp; The Federal income tax, now entirely phased out, is recognized as a relic of a bygone age.</p>
<p>A vision of this theoretical 2025 America presents itself.&nbsp; The economic climate at all levels of American society is fundamentally transformed.&nbsp; Careers are plentiful and compensation is more closely aligned with productivity than before.&nbsp; Entrepreneurship has become more widespread and lucrative, and is now the largest occupation, with clusters of tech startups sprouting in all cities.&nbsp; Inflation is minor, and many products cost the same or less than they did in 2020, particularly when adjusting for ATOM impact on product quality.&nbsp; Debates about living wages, minimum wages, federal tax hikes, and two-income traps have vanished into the dustbin of history.&nbsp; Even if state, local, sales, and property taxes still exist, the burden seems minor, and governance in most state and city governments has improved, partly due to a reduction in some categories of crime, and the government resources freed up through this.</p>
<p>At the highest macroeconomic level, the US National Debt, which has recently crossed the psychologically significant threshold of 100% of GDP for the first time in a non-war economy, ceases to grow since government spending no longer generates a deficit funded by US Treasury debt.&nbsp; The $23 Trillion of existing Treasury bills, notes, and bonds will shrink as each individual debt contract matures and expires without being replaced with new debt contracts from a new deficit.&nbsp; This eventually makes the entire debt, which is going to be a major factor in exacerbating the pain of the next eventual recession as previously described, gradually shrink and disappear.&nbsp; Thus, the supposedly ominous National Debt and budget deficits become a non-factors in future fiscal policy structuring under this model.</p>
<p>Not every problem dwindles away, of course.&nbsp; If America of 2020 has proven anything, it is that some people just cannot handle prosperity.&nbsp; When serious problems recede away, they devote their lives towards inventing new ones, as the endlessly mutating victim-chic culture demonstrates.&nbsp; Some people will always concoct new grievances as a cloak through which they resentfully harass others, and most of the tired old shibboleths will continue.&nbsp; What may be different is that AI may greatly empower the efforts of factseekers, enabling them to obstruct the grievance extortionists more effectively than is possible now.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<p>This is where the second-choice program, the Sovereign Venture Fund (SVF), becomes an alternative.&nbsp; Since the first-mover country is effectively capturing the entire world's ATOM deflation for domestic benefit, it should have its central bank generate a full $1 Trillion (possible $2 Trillion), and keep <em>it in a segregated account and out of the domestic economy</em>, as this actually would cause inflation if it circulated.&nbsp; Then, before another country becomes aware of this, deploy it into overseas allocations (equities, real estate, etc.) as quickly as possible.&nbsp; Hence, these are positions now owned by the SVF, and can then be more precisely deployed into projects or technologies that specifically hedge domestic risk.&nbsp; This can make far more speculative bets than a Sovereign Wealth Fund, which was built through decades of taxpayer money and understandably wants to be more risk averse.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<p>As I have stressed, no country will even begin to implement this plan in the immediate future, and even then, the US will definitely not be the first to embark on this path.&nbsp; But there are four countries with unique attributes that enable them to be the first to reap the benefits of this approach to fiscal and monetary governance.&nbsp; These four countries, which happen to be four of the best-managed economies in the world, consist of two single-party Pacific Rim city-states, Hong Kong and Singapore, and two Western democracies, Canada and Switzerland.</p>
<p><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p><strong>Hong Kong and Singapore : </strong>&nbsp;Hong Kong and Singapore have shared origins that led them to become &nbsp;city-state tax havens with small, efficient governments.&nbsp; Both have per-capita GDPs that are manifold higher than the countries they were separated from (China and Malaysia, respectively), and their economies have high ATOM densities.&nbsp; Both are routinely ranked within the top four financial centers in the world, despite having been quite poor as recently as the 1970s.</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_government_budgets_by_country">Each of these city-states has a government budget of about $60 Billion/year</a></span>.&nbsp; As small nations with very high interconnectedness to much larger economies, they could each fund their governments with central bank money, waive all of their already-low taxes, and still be just a rounding error in the world monetary and inflation data.&nbsp; In fact, the inflation rate of Hong Kong and Singapore is more determined by whatever monetary expansion is done by the big central banks of China, Japan, and the US.&nbsp; As a result, both Hong Kong and Singapore can proceed with the full knowledge that their money creation will not cause any significant local inflation or currency devaluation.&nbsp; Once they fund their existing government budgets, they can proceed on their own program to phase out taxes and disburse a DUES, which itself can rise at a faster rate than a larger country could manage.</p>
<p>Hong Kong and Singapore are not democracies, so their political process does not double as an entertainment genre.&nbsp; Hence, the sequence of decisions involved in this sort of restructuring can be made and executed quickly.&nbsp; Due to currency pegs, a currency flight is not a risk.&nbsp; The stipend may have to start small, but can rapidly scale up at speeds a large country could not manage.&nbsp; The stipend could reach $50,000/year within five years, without triggering any domestic inflation.&nbsp; They could, similarly, implement the SVF immediately, at a size of $1 Trillion or more.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<p>Decades of low-tax, business-friendly policies have cultivated a culture of governance that makes them among the least likely governments to get carried away in a frenzy of ‘free money’ gluttony.&nbsp; Whatever approvals Hong Kong needs from China are unlikely to be a problem given China’s own massive and innovative monetary practices, and inclination to observe how well the Hong Kong experiment works.&nbsp; Success in Hong Kong and Singapore can clarify a roadmap for other countries in the region that could already implement the strategy (Taiwan, South Korea, and Japan), and eventually, the largest practitioner of ATOM monetization in the future (China).</p>
<p><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p><strong>Canada and Switzerland :</strong>&nbsp; Among Western democracies, there are two very different countries that share the distinction of being the best positioned to transition to the Third Millennium Economics paradigm of fiscal and monetary governance.&nbsp; Traditional disadvantages can now be converted to advantages, in a historic turning of tables.</p>
<p>Canada is in the unique situation of having a sole geographical neighbor with an economy ten times larger, with which Canada conducts most of its trade.&nbsp; Given the high ATOM density of both nations, the inflation rate in Canada can never deviate significantly from that of the United States.&nbsp; At first, it may seem troubling that a major aspect of the Canadian economy is predominantly pegged to how America performs on the same measure.&nbsp; But from what we have seen about the potential re-direction of central bank monetary easing, Canada is superbly well-positioned.</p>
<p>Most Americans are not aware that Canada’s federal government does not typically run a budget deficit.&nbsp; The Canadian government, as of 2019, <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2019_Canadian_federal_budget">collects about $340 Billion in tax revenue, and spends a slightly higher $355 Billion in an impressive demonstration of political restraint</a></span>.&nbsp; The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_public_debt">national debt of Canada is only about one third the proportional size of the US debt</a>, a trajectory quite the opposite of the one the US is currently on.</p>
<p>If the Canadian Parliament were to authorize the <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bank_of_Canada">Canadian central bank</a></span> to merely create the entire $355 Billion of federal expenditures and waive all federal income taxes, the amount of monetary expansion is negligible relative to the broader US money supply.&nbsp; This ensures that there is no possibility of any Canada-specific inflation.&nbsp; It really is that simple, amidst Canada’s favorable conditions (Canada also happens to have a more skill-based immigration policy <a href="http://www.npr.org/2015/05/26/409671996/canada-cuts-down-on-red-tape-could-it-work-in-the-u-s"><span style="text-decoration: underline;">and better regulatory philosophy than the US at present</span></a>, all of which strengthen the ATOM levels in the country).</p>
<p>Canadian provinces have taxes and budgets of their own, of course, but the entire federal government is already well within the zone where taxation of Canadian citizens is no longer necessary.&nbsp; Due to this, a DUES program can be constructed in short order without generating any discernible inflation in either Canada or the US.&nbsp; For any Canadian reading this, perhaps you should send this reading material to your elected representatives and start urging them to examine these ideas.</p>
<p>Switzerland, by contrast, has arrived at this juncture along a very different path than Canada.&nbsp; Few countries have done more things correctly for as many centuries as Switzerland, which built an economy entirely around high-margin industries and is synonymous with an elite image of wealth and sophistication.&nbsp; Switzerland is not part of the Eurozone currency block, but is completely surrounded by it.&nbsp; The Eurozone economy is about 16 times larger than the Swiss economy, and most of the Eurozone’s GDP is generated within 300 kilometers of the Swiss border, representing a huge ATOM-deflationary sink.&nbsp; This guarantees the Swiss central bank’s ability to generate an almost unlimited amount of money without causing any domestic inflation.</p>
<p>Above and beyond the extremely suitable geographical location, <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://www.bbc.com/news/business-25415501">Switzerland has already considered experimenting with the idea of a universal stipend</a></span> (<span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-33977636">as has Finland</a></span>).&nbsp; The only missing piece is to have the stipend paid with money created by the Swiss central bank, rather than by the large tax increase they were considering.&nbsp; As the stream of money increases, the rest of the government budget can draw from this source, while all Swiss income taxes can be gradually eliminated.</p>
<p>Excellent governance, high technology density, their own central banks, and proximity to vastly larger economies with low inflation will hopefully lead Canada and Switzerland to become the first to undergo the legislative and political process of implementing this solution, setting an example for the larger Western democracies to follow.&nbsp; Similar to the example of New Zealand provided in Chapter 7, Canada or Switzerland could also implement a $1 Trillion SVF in the same way, provided one of them moves first.&nbsp; The US and Eurozone will only implement such programs when they already see it succeeding in a smaller Western nation, and that too after the first-mover advantage is no longer available.</p>
<p>Continue to :&nbsp;<span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://atom.singularity2050.com/11-implementation-of-the-atom-age-for-individuals.html">11. Implementation of Third Millennium Economics for Individuals</a></span></p>
</div>
