AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: 1. Prologue
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: 1-prologue

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/1-prologue.html 
DATE: 06/06/2016 12:09:26 AM
-----
BODY:
<p style="text-align: center;">&nbsp;</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><em>Time is the fire in which we burn.</em></p>
<p style="text-align: right;">– Delmore Schwartz</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=tUTtNHw7VgU">(Video)</a></span>. &nbsp;I once had a dream in midsummer, 2015.&nbsp; I was in a dark and humid basement one afternoon, and I came across a very large book.&nbsp; Upon slapping away the dust from the cover of this grand tome, its title became visible, a title both grand and vague – <em>Exponential and Accelerating</em>.&nbsp; The mildew and centipedes aside, this was just too interesting to pass up.&nbsp; I hastily proceeded to peruse through its yellowed pages to see what was contained within; to imbibe the compilation of events from eons long past to see how we got to today.</p>
<p>The first few chapters of the book described the origin of the Earth, the evolution of early life and the progress through the geologic periods.&nbsp; The arrival of multicellular creatures, vertebrates, and advanced animals was described, but what stood out was the unstated subtext that the rate of evolutionary change was inherently accelerating, and that this accelerating gradient has been present all along, and evident at all points from 4.5 billion years ago to the present.&nbsp; It is almost as though there is an inherent recursive loop of acceleration within the genetic code of all life.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<p><a class="asset-img-link" style="float: left;" href="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e202c8d3b18e15200c-pi"><img class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e202c8d3b18e15200c img-responsive" alt="Picture5" title="Picture5" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e202c8d3b18e15200c-320wi" style="margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;" /></a>The first full 88% of the time since the origin of life on Earth represented very little advancement relative to what happened immediately thereafter, described as the '<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cambrian_explosion">Cambrian Explosion</a>' 540 million years ago.&nbsp; But it was an 'explosion' only to the extent that a linear perception would depict it as such.&nbsp; Under a logarithmic scale, it was right on schedule.&nbsp; Additionally, the rate of change was faster from that point onward; there was never a return to the pre-Cambrian slower rate of evolution.&nbsp; Even the subsequent dinosaurs of the Mesozoic era, which we think of as having lived very long ago, existed 95-98.5% of the way to the present in a linear time scale since the origin of life.&nbsp; Notice how each era becomes smaller in duration, on account of the accelerating rate of change inserting a rising level of evolution in each unit of time closer to the present.&nbsp; The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cenozoic">Cenozoic era</a> is the smallest, since it is the closest to the present, yet has the greatest differential of life forms between its start to the present, with the differential itself weighted to the present.&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</p>
<p><a class="asset-img-link" style="float: right;" href="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20263e94a582d200b-pi"><img class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20263e94a582d200b img-responsive" alt="Human Brain Trendline" title="Human Brain Trendline" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20264e2dc6ef0200d-pi" style="margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;" width="256" height="297" /></a>Then, it became apparent that the entire description of evolution through to protohumans only occupied the first small fraction of this book, even if that was 99.9% of the elapsed time.&nbsp; The emergence of human intelligence (see illustration) occurred within an extremely brief period relative to the entire evolutionary record, almost as though intelligence was exponential.&nbsp; It took 4.5 billion years for something as intelligent as a chimpanzee to evolve, and then it took just 5 million more years for the size of the brain to triple.&nbsp; After that, the entire existence of modern humans was yet another miniscule fraction of that period.&nbsp; It seemed that the events of the last 40,000 years were more worthy of prose than the events of the preceding 4.5 billion years.</p>
<p>On to the Age of Man, I read about the earliest agriculture, the great ancient civilizations, and continued on through the Middle Ages.&nbsp; It was apparent that each century occupied more pages of the book, or rather, that each century was more filled with noteworthy events.&nbsp; The last five centuries received the most detail, and the twentieth century itself had more content than the entirety of eons prior to that point.&nbsp; I continued to read on about the Industrial Revolution, the beginnings of space exploration, and the computer age.&nbsp; The common theme was that we live in a time where events occur at an increasingly rapid rate.</p>
<p>Yet, I was only halfway through the book when I reached the present, and this puzzled me.&nbsp; I looked at the copyright date of the book and was stunned.&nbsp; The book in my dream was written in the early 22<sup>nd</sup> century, and the remaining few hundred pages were a description of the 21<sup>st</sup> century!&nbsp; I could barely contain my excitement at the prospect of reading the second half of this volume, but the pages seemed to be welded shut, and I could not turn to the pages past what described my present day.&nbsp; I tried to pry it open with a screwdriver, but to no avail.&nbsp; My dream did not allow me to see the most interesting section of this book from the future.&nbsp; I could only ponder the profound possibility that the latter 85 (now 80 as of 2020) years of the century were so eventful that it occupied half of the book; the same length as all of Earth’s noteworthy events from the start until 2015.</p>
<p>I awoke suddenly, and the next morning went straight to the bookstore to peruse some of the latest books on futurism.&nbsp; A few volumes from renowned thinkers in various fields occupied the shelves, some on the future of artificial intelligence, other books on space exploration, and still others on biotechnology.&nbsp; However, each of those books described a future saturated predominantly with only their specified technology, thereby making each book mutually exclusive with the others.&nbsp; The more holistic and multidisciplinary books were over a decade old, a situation entirely inconsistent with the accelerating rate of technological progress.&nbsp; Lastly, there was little in the way of practical applications such as policy recommendations for economic and political leaders, or guidance for ordinary people seeking to adapt to this tsunami of technological change.&nbsp; Many questions of contemporary importance were left unaddressed.</p>
<p>This vacuum convinced me that the time had come for a compact publication that weaves these rapidly accelerating but seemingly disparate strands into a single tapestry of our destiny.&nbsp; To transcend the mere theoretical, the publication must provide multiple solutions for the increasingly stifling bottleneck created by the outdated econo-political apparatus.&nbsp; For ages, the default assumptions have been built around tradeoffs between safety nets and higher taxes, or guaranteed minimum incomes and business friendliness.&nbsp; But times are changing, and we are on the brink of an era where technological diffusion will be pervasive and pronounced enough to make some of these tradeoffs recede.&nbsp; The churn of fortunes and prosperity will increasingly be governed by how much an individual, business, or government grasps the concepts of exponential, accelerating economic and technological progress.</p>
<p>There comes a rare time when a seemingly unrelated tangle of very complicated problems that continues to vex all established assumptions can be addressed with a comprehensive, elegant, yet simple solution.&nbsp; If ever there was a time and place for ‘outside the box’ ideas of grand scope, it is here and now.&nbsp; As a society, we could be on the brink of taking prosperity to a new level with some remarkably straightforward economic and monetary adaptations.&nbsp; But if we do not embark on these reforms, we will soon have another financial crisis within the next few years, which may be worse than the previous one.</p>
<p>To describe the multiple interlocking forces between technology, economics, finance, and government in as simple and concise a manner as possible, I have embedded brief introductory videos at the start of major chapters.&nbsp; This layer is for the benefit of those who would like a summary of a particular topic in the publication before they decide to read a formidable wall of text.&nbsp; The goal of this publication is to reach a large and diverse audience of people on some very complicated subject matter, among whom learning speeds and styles can vary greatly.</p>
<p>What you are about to read and watch might change the way you look at the world, and fully change every assumption you have about the future, mostly for the better.&nbsp; At a minimum, you might never look at certain slices of life the same way again.</p>
<p>To begin, let us first consider the many complicated trends, policies, and interconnections that govern the world today.&nbsp; Perhaps you are worried about technology replacing your job or the jobs of people close to you.&nbsp; Perhaps you feel that your taxes are too high, and that government spending patterns do not reflect your values.&nbsp; Perhaps you are troubled as to why interest rates are nearly zero, yet there seems to be deflation spreading across many sectors of the economy.&nbsp; Perhaps you just feel that technology is creating a new type of inequality that is hard to describe by old-fashioned criteria, yet palpable on an instinctual level.</p>
<p>If you feel any or all of the above questions are a source of personal uncertainty, then this is the reading material for you.&nbsp; You may start thinking about many important topics that have very little written about them.&nbsp; Even better, perhaps you can get behind some of the ideas I have presented here, since many of these challenges can be addressed in highly complementary ways.&nbsp; As we embark on this unpacking process, we must divide the body of knowledge into sections.&nbsp; There are multiple concepts that tie together to form the grand unified set of analyses and recommendations I am presenting here.</p>
<p>To begin, we will first establish the case that economic growth is and always has been exponential and accelerating, and has been throughout all of human civilization, even as it is being partly stifled at present.&nbsp; Secondly, we will examine the deepening scope of technological penetration into an ever-widening share of the economy, how this is creating accelerating deflation, and why this is not necessarily a negative thing.&nbsp; After that, we will arrive at a policy solution for governments and central banks to assess and implement, designed to remove the drag effect of the current set of policies and set the stage for the next era of economic ascendance.&nbsp; Finally, we will detail some case studies and ideas that you can benefit from on a personal level, and claim a greater slice of the Third Millennium Economy, since, after all, some others already are.</p>
<p>Continue to :&nbsp;<span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="http://atom.singularity2050.com/2-the-exponential-trendline-of-economic-growth.html">2. The Exponential Trendline of Economic Growth</a></span></p>

AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: Third Millennium Economics
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: executive-summary

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2016/02/executive-summary.html 
DATE: 02/26/2016 06:34:00 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>&#0160;</p>
<p>Welcome. &#0160;This is a book/whitepaper published in website format (<span class="asset  asset-generic at-xid-6a00d83452455969e202c8d3b3ea3c200c img-responsive"><a href="https://futurist.typepad.com/files/atom-36.pdf">as well as a .pdf</a></span>), about how technology has created new economic forces that are reshaping society. &#0160;Contained within is guidance on how individuals, organizations, and nations have to adapt to these transformations in order to prosper.&#0160;</p>
<p>If you are interested in any of the following, this is the reading material for you :</p>
<ul>
<li>Accelerating, exponential technological progress</li>
<li>Technological disruption, and its social and economic impact</li>
<li>How artificial intelligence and robotics are affecting employment</li>
<li>Why most mainstream economists are unable to measure the economic impact of technology, and no longer understand monetary forces</li>
<li>Navigating the next recession/financial crisis</li>
<li>Creating a dynamic safety net that replaces outdated government programs, and is simultaneously the destination of central bank easing</li>
<li>A path towards migrating the tax burden away from humans and onto technological productivity</li>
<li>How a country can hedge against technological disruption of its core industries</li>
<li>How the data you generate can be monetized for your benefit</li>
<li>How to harness technological forces for yourself</li>
</ul>
<p>I hope this content changes how you view the world, in the present and the future. &#0160;</p>
<p>&#0160;</p>
<p style="text-align: right;"><a class="asset-img-link" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e201b8d1f732d1970c-pi" style="float: left;"><img alt="KartikPhoto" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e201b8d1f732d1970c img-responsive" height="42" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e201b8d1f732d1970c-120wi" style="margin: 0px 5px 5px 0px;" title="KartikPhoto" width="33" /></a>– <span style="text-decoration: underline;"><a href="https://www.linkedin.com/in/kartikgada">Kartik Gada</a></span></p>
<p style="text-align: right;">Version 1.0 : February 26, 2016<a href="https://technomedium.wordpress.com/"></a></p>
<p style="text-align: right;">Version 2.0 : <span style="text-decoration: line-through;">February 29, 2020</span></p>
<p style="text-align: right;">May 31, 2020</p>
<p style="text-align: right;">&#0160;</p>
<hr />
<p style="text-align: center;">&#0160;</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><strong>Executive Summary</strong></p>
<p><span style="color: #007f40;">The accelerating pace and diffusion of technological change has taken control of an ever-growing fraction of the world economy.&#0160; This fraction is being assimilated into a different set of economic fundamentals, such as the rapid and exponential price deflation inherent to technology.&#0160; The effect of this was insignificant until recently, but is now beginning to create conspicuous distortions in many economic metrics, and is just years from being the dominant force across the entire economy.&#0160;</span></p>
<p><span style="color: #007f40;">In response to technological deflation, the central banks of the world will have to create new money in perpetuity, increasing the stream at an exponentially rising rate much higher than is currently assumed.&#0160; This now-permanent need for monetary expansion, if embraced, can fund government spending more directly.&#0160; This in turn creates a very robust, dynamic, and efficient safety net for citizens, while simultaneously reducing and even eliminating most forms of taxation by 2025-30.&#0160; Alternatively, this monetary expansion can be a means to create Sovereign Venture Funds that can hedge a country&#39;s risk far more effectively than existing sovereign wealth funds.&#0160;&#0160;</span></p>
<p><span style="color: #007f40;">Failure to recognize that technological deflation mandates permanent and ever-rising central bank monetary expansion that can and should gradually become the primary source of government spending could result in countries falling behind more enlightened countries in a very short time.&#0160;</span></p>
<p><span style="color: #007f40;">The nature of current worldwide technology is to link various disruptions with each other, consume monetary liquidity to generate deflation, and lower the effective prices of most goods and services over time.&#0160; Therefore, the entirety of worldwide technology has to be seen as a holistic economic entity, and can be defined as an ‘Accelerating TechnOnomic Medium’, or ‘ATOM’, which itself is the central concept of &#39;Third Millennium Economics&#39;.&#0160;&#0160;</span></p>
<p>&#0160;</p>
<hr />
<h5>&#0160;</h5>

AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: Carbonara
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: carbonara
CATEGORY: Accelerating Change
CATEGORY: Computing
CATEGORY: Consumer Technology
CATEGORY: Energy
CATEGORY: Nanotechnology
CATEGORY: Science
CATEGORY: Technological Breakthroughs

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2010/11/carbonara.html 
DATE: 11/06/2010 12:51:42 PM
-----
BODY:
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt; font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva;">Observers have been waiting for carbon nanotubes, buckyballs, and graphene to transform the world for quite some time, and the wait has been longer than they expected.&#0160; Enthusiasts for this new miracle material had all but vanished.&#0160; Is this warranted?&#0160; Where does the state of innovation in various forms of carbon, that could yield ultra-strong, ultra-light materials and superfast computing&#0160;really stand?&#0160;</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 10pt;">CNET had <a href="http://news.cnet.com/8301-11128_3-20017972-54.html">an article just last month&#0160;about the multiple disruptions that the various allotopes of carbon are about to make</a>.&#0160; That is quite exciting, except that <a href="http://news.cnet.com/The-stuff-of-dreams/2009-1008_3-5091267.html">CNET also had a similar article in 2003</a>.&#0160; Similarly, Ray Kurzweil&#0160;extolled carbon nanotubes as a successor to silicon quite&#0160;heavily in 1999, but not quite as much now, even though that supposed transition would be much closer to the present.&#0160; This does not mean that Kurzweil&#39;s estimation was in error, but rather that the technology was unexpectedly stagnant during the early 2000s.&#0160;&#0160;So let us examine why there was such an interruption, and whether progress has since resumed.&#0160; &#0160;</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 10pt;"><a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/03/nanotechnology-bubble-bustboom.html" style="float: right;" target="_self"><img alt="Graphene" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20133f57c20f1970b" height="221" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20133f57c20f1970b-320wi" style="margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;" title="Graphene" width="296" /></a></span><br /><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 10pt;"> I wrote <a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/03/nanotechnology-bubble-bustboom.html" target="_self">in 2009 about how we had undergone a multi-year nanotech winter, and how we were emerging from it in 2009</a>.&#0160; As anticipated, carbon nanotubes are now finally lowering in price, and being produced at a scale that could start making an impact.&#0160; Sure enough, activity began to stir right as I predicted, and t<a href="http://www.technologyreview.com/computing/26469/?p1=A4&amp;a=f">he 2010 Nobel Prize in physics has been awarded to research in graphene</a>.&#0160; Just like CNETs article, <a href="http://www.wired.com/gadgetlab/2010/10/graphene/" target="_self">Wired also has an article about the diverse applications that graphene could revolutionize</a>.&#0160; Combining the two articles, we can summarize&#0160;the core&#0160;possibilities of carbon allotopes as follows&#0160;:</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 10pt;"><strong>Ultra-dense computing and storage </strong>: Graphene transistors smaller than 1 nanometer have been demonstrated.&#0160; Carbon allotopes could keep the exponential doubling of both computing and storage&#0160;capacity going well into the 2030s.&#0160;</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 10pt;"><strong>Carbon Fiber Vehicles </strong>: This lightweight, ultrastrong material can save vast amounts of fuel by reducing the weight of cars and aeroplanes.&#0160; While premium products such as the $6000 Trek Madone bicycles are already made from carbon fiber, greater volume is reducing prices and will soon make the average car much ligher than it is today, increasing fuel efficiency and reducing traffic fatalities.&#0160;</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 10pt;"><strong>Energy Storage :</strong> Natural Gas is not only much cheaper than oil per unit of energy (oil would have to drop to about $30 to match current NG prices), but the supply of NG is more evenly distributed across the world than the oil supply.&#0160; The US alone has an enormous reserve of natural gas that could ensure total energy independence.&#0160; The main problem with NG is storage, which is the primary reason oil displacement is not happening rapidly.&#0160; But microporous carbon can effectively act as a sponge for natural gas, enabling safe and easy transport.&#0160; This could potentially change the entire energy map.</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 10pt;">There are other applications beyond these core three, but suffice it to say, the allotopes of carbon can perform a greater variety of functions than any other material available to us today.&#0160; Watch for indications of carbon allotopes popping up in the strangest of places, and know that each emergence drives the cost down ever lower.&#0160;</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 10pt;">Related :</span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 10pt;"><a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/03/nanotechnology-bubble-bustboom.html" target="_self">Nanotechnology : Bubble, Bust,.....Boom?</a></span></p>
<p><span style="font-family: &#39;trebuchet ms&#39;, geneva; font-size: 11pt;"><a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/04/mili_micro_nano.html" target="_self">Milli, Micro, Nano, Pico</a></span></p>

AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: The Techno-Sponge
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: the-technosponge
CATEGORY: Accelerating Change
CATEGORY: Computing
CATEGORY: Consumer Technology
CATEGORY: Economics
CATEGORY: The Singularity

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2010/07/the-technosponge.html 
DATE: 07/06/2010 10:29:33 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">After years of thinking about this, I have come up with a term that can describe the thoughts I have had about the new, 'good' type of deflation that is evading the notice of almost all of the top economists in the world today.&nbsp; This changes many of the most fundamental assumptions about economics, even as most economic thought is far behind the curve.&nbsp; </P>
<P>First, let us review some events that transpired over the last 2 years.&nbsp; To stave off the prospect of a deflationary spiral that could lead to a depression, the major governments of the world followed 20th-century textbook economics, and injected colossal amounts of liquidity into the financial system.&nbsp; In the US, not only was the Fed Funds rate lowered to nearly zero (for now 18 months and counting), but an additional $1 Trillion was injected in.&nbsp; </P>
<P>However, now that a depression has been averted, and the recession has ended, we were supposed to experience inflation even amidst high unemployment, just like we did in the 1970s, to minimize debt burdens.&nbsp; But alas, there is still no inflation, despite a yield curve with more than 3% steepness, and a near-0% FF rate for so long.&nbsp; How could this be?&nbsp; What is absorbing all the liquidity?&nbsp; &nbsp; </P>
<P>In&nbsp;<A href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/04/the-impact-of-computing-78-more-each-year.html#tp">The Impact of Computing</A>, I discussed how 1.5% of World GDP today comprises of products where the same functionality can be purchased for a price that halves every 18 months.&nbsp; 'Moore's Law' applies to semiconductors, but storage, software, and some biotech are also on a similar exponential curve.&nbsp; This force makes productivity gains higher, and inflation lower, than traditional 20th century economics would anticipate.&nbsp; Furthermore, the second derivative is also increasing - the rate of productivity gains itself is accelerating.&nbsp;&nbsp;1.5% of World GDP may be small, but what about when this percentage grows to 3% of World GDP?&nbsp; 5%?&nbsp; We may only be a decade away from this, and the impact of this technological deflation will be more obvious.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Most high-tech companies have a business model that incorporates a sort of 'bizarro force' that is completely the opposite of what old-economy companies operate under :&nbsp;<em>The price of the products sold by a high-tech company decreases over time</em>.&nbsp; Any other company will manage inventory, pricing, and forecasts under an assumption of inflationary price increases, but a technology company exists under the reality that all inventory depreciates very quickly (at over 10% per quarter in many cases), and that price drops will shrink revenues unless unit sales rise enough to offset it (and assuming that enough unit inventory was even produced).&nbsp; This results in the constant pressure to create new and improved products every few months just to occupy prime price points, without which revenues would plunge within just a year.&nbsp; Yet, high-tech companies have built hugely profitable businesses around these peculiar challenges, and at least&nbsp;8 such US companies have market capitalizations over $100 Billion.&nbsp; 6 of those 8 are headquartered in Silicon Valley.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Now, here is the point to ponder : We have never had a significant technology sector while also facing the&nbsp;fears (warranted or otherwise)&nbsp;of high inflation.&nbsp; When high inflation vanished in 1982, the technology sector was too tiny to be considered a significant contributor to macroeconomic statistics.&nbsp; In an environment of high inflation combined with a large technology industry, however, major consumer retail pricepoints, such as $99.99 or $199.99, become more affordable.&nbsp; The same also applies to enterprise-class customers.&nbsp; Thus, demand creeps upwards even as cost to produce the products goes down on the same Impact of Computing curve.&nbsp; <em>This allows a technology company the ability to postpone price drops and expand margins, or to sell more volume at the same nominal dollar price.&nbsp; </em>Hence, higher inflation causes the revenues and/or margins of technology companies to rise, which means their earnings-per-share certainly surges.</P>
<P>So what we are seeing is the gigantic amount of liquidity created by the Federal Reserve is instead cycling through technology companies and increasing their earnings.&nbsp; <em>The products they sell, in turn, increase productivity and promptly push inflation back down.</em>&nbsp; Every uptick in inflation merely guarantees its own pushback, and the 1.5% of GDP that mops up all the liquidity and creates this form of 'good' deflation can be termed as the 'Techno-Sponge'.&nbsp; So how much liquidity can&nbsp;the Techno-Sponge&nbsp;absorb before saturation?&nbsp; </P>
<P>At this point, if the US prints another $1 Trillion, that will still merely halt deflation, and there will be no hint of inflation at all.&nbsp; It would take a full&nbsp;$2 Trillion to saturate the techno-sponge, and temporarily push consumer inflation to even the less-than-terrifying level of 4% while also generating substantial jumps in productivity and tech company earnings.&nbsp; In fact, the demographics of the US, with baby boomers reaching their geriatric years, are highly deflationary (and this is the bad type of deflation), so the US would have to print another $1 Trillion every year for the next 10 years just to offset demographic deflation, and keep the techno-sponge saturated.&nbsp; </P>
<P>A techno-sponge that is 1.5% of GDP might be keeping CPI inflation at under 2%, but when the techno-sponge is 3% of GDP, even trillions of dollars of liquidity won't halt deflation.&nbsp; Deflation may become normal, even as living standards and productivity rise at ever-increasing rates.&nbsp; The people who will suffer are holders of debt, particularly mortgage debt.&nbsp; Inflating away debt will no longer be a tool available to rescue people (and governments) from their errors.&nbsp; The biggest beneficiaries will be technology companies, and those who are tied to them.&nbsp; </P>
<P>But to keep prosperity rising, productivity has to rise at the maximum possible rate.&nbsp; This requires the Techno-Sponge to be kept full at all times - the 'new normal'.&nbsp; Thus, the printing press has to start on the first $1 Trillion now, and printing has to continue <em>until</em> we see inflation.&nbsp;&nbsp;Economists will be surprised at how much can be printed without seeing any inflation, and will not be able to draw the connection about why the printed money is boosting productivity.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Related :</P>
<P><A href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/04/the-impact-of-computing-78-more-each-year.html#tp">The Impact of Computing</A></P>
<P><A href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/09/timing-the-singularity.html">Timing the Singularity</A></P></span>
-----

TITLE: The Carnival of Creative Destruction
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: the-carnival-of-creative-destruction
CATEGORY: Accelerating Change
CATEGORY: Biotechnology
CATEGORY: Computing
CATEGORY: Consumer Technology
CATEGORY: Energy
CATEGORY: Internet
CATEGORY: Technological Breakthroughs

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2010/05/the-carnival-of-creative-destruction.html 
DATE: 05/20/2010 10:49:44 PM
-----
BODY:
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;">Words like &#39;disruption&#39; and &#39;destruction&#39; usually have negative meanings, and one may strain to find any good ways in which to use the terms.&#0160; But today, the <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/12/are_you_acceler.html">accelerating rate of change</a> ensures that more technologies alter more aspects of life at an ever-quickening rate.&#0160; A little-understood dimension of this is the concept of Joseph Schumpeter&#39;s &#39;Creative Destruction&#39;, where the process of technological change topples existing norms, often quite rapidly.&#0160; </span></p>
<p><span style="font-size: 10pt;"><a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2008/02/is-technology-d.html">Technological diffusion was in a lull in 2008, as I pointed out at the time</a>.&#0160; But now, in 2010, I am happy to report that the recess has passed, and that the accelerating rate of change is rising back to the long-term exponential trendline (although it may not be fully back at the trendline until 2013, when people who have not been paying attention will be wondering why they were taken by surprise).&#0160; The <a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/04/the-impact-of-computing-78-more-each-year.html">Impact of Computing</a> continues to progress, infusing itself into a wider and wider swath of our lives, and speeding up the rate of change in complacently stagnant industries that never thought technology could affect them.&#0160; Silicon Valley continues to be &#39;ground zero&#39; for creative destruction, and complacent industries thousands of miles away could be toppled by someone working from their bedroom in Silicon Valley.&#0160;</span></p>
<p>Just a few of the examples of creative destruction that is presently in process have been covered by prior articles here at Techsector.&#0160; These, along with others, are :</p>
<p>1)&#0160;Video Conferencing&#0160;is poised to disrupt not just airline and hotel industry revenues (which stand to lose tens of billions of dollars per year of business travel revenue), but the real-estate, medical, and&#0160;aeronautical industries as well.&#0160; Corporations will see substantial productivity gains from successful adoption of videoconferencing as a substitute for 50% or more of their travel expenses.&#0160; Major mergers and acquisitions have happened in this sector in the last few months, and imminent price reductions will open the floodgates of diffusion.&#0160; Skype provides a form of video telephony that is free of cost.&#0160; <a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/08/video-conferencing-a-cascade-of-disruptions.html">This is described in detail in my August 2008 article on the subject</a>, as well as in my earlier <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/10/ciscos_hd_video.html">October 2006 introductory article</a>.&#0160;</p>
<p>2) Surface Computing, <a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2008/07/surface-interfaces-the-next-killer-ap-in-computing.html">which I wrote about in July of 2008</a>, has begun to emerge in a myriad of forms, from the handheld Apple iPad to the upcoming consumer version of the table-sized Microsoft Surface.&#0160; This not only transforms human-computer interaction for the first time in&#0160;decades, but the Apple &#39;Apps&#39; ecosystem&#0160;alters the utility of the Internet as well.&#0160; All sizes between the blackboard and the iPad will soon be available, and by 2015, personal computing, and the Internet, will be quite different than they are today, with surfaces of varying sizes abundant in many homes.&#0160;</p>
<p>3)&#0160;The complete and total transformation of video games into the dominant form of home entertainment will be visible by 2012 through a combination of technologies such as realistic graphics, motion-responsive controllers, 3-D televisions, voice recognition, etc.&#0160; The biggest casualty of this disruption will be television programming, which will struggle to retain viewers.&#0160; Beyond this, the way in which humans process sensations of pleasure, excitement, and entertainment will irrevocably change.&#0160; Thus, the way humans relate to each other will also change.&#0160; <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/04/the_next_big_th.html">I have written about this in April 2006</a>, with <a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/07/the-next-big-thing-in-entertainment-a-halftime-update.html">a follow-up in July 2009</a>.&#0160;</p>
<p>4) The book-publishing industry has been stubbornly resistant to technology, as evidenced by their insistence as late as 2003 that manuscript queries be submitted by postal mail, and that a self-addressed stamped envelope&#0160;be enclosed in which a reply can be sent.&#0160; A completed manuscript would take a full 12 months to be printed and distributed, and the editors didn&#39;t even find this to be odd.&#0160; Fortunately, two simultaneous disruptions are toppling this obsolete and unproductive industry from both ends.&#0160; Print-on-demand services that greatly shorten the self-publishing process and entry-cost, such as iUniverse and Blurb, are now flexible and easy, while finished books can further avoid the paper-binding process altogether and be available to millions in e-book format for the Kindle and other e-readers.&#0160; When&#0160;e-readers&#0160;are eventually available for only $100, bookstores that sell paper books will be relegated to surviving mostly on gifts, coffee table books, and cafe revenues.&#0160; This is a disruption that is happening quickly due to it being so overdue in the first place, resulting in a speedy &#39;catchup&#39;.&#0160; <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2009/12/the-publishing-disruption.html">I wrote about this in more detail in December of 2009</a>.</p>
<p>5)&#0160;<a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/05/a_future_timeli.html">The automobile is undergoing multiple major transformations at once</a>.&#0160; Strong, light nanomaterials are entering the bodies of&#0160;cars to increase fuel efficiency, engines are migrating to hybrid and electrical forms, sub-$5000 cars in India and China will lead to innovations that percolate up to lower the cost of traditional Western models, and the computational power engineered into the average car today&#0160;leads to major feature jumps&#0160;relative to models from just 5 years ago.&#0160; The $25,000 car of 2020 will be superior to the $50,000 car of 2005 is every measurable way.&#0160;</p>
<p>By 2016, consumer behavior will change to a mode where people consider it normal to &#39;upgrade&#39; their perfectly functioning 6-year-old cars to get a newer model with better electronic features.&#0160;&#0160;This may seem odd, but people did not&#0160;tend to replace&#0160;fully functional television sets before they failed until the 2003 thin-TV disruption.&#0160; The Impact of Computing pulls ever-more products into a rapid trajectory of improvement.&#0160;</p>
<p>By 2018, self-driving cars will be readily available to the average US consumer, and will constitute a significant fraction of cars on the highway.&#0160; This will&#0160;revise existing assumptions about&#0160;highway speeds and acceptable commute distances, and will further impede the real estate prices of expensive areas.&#0160;</p>
<p>6) The Mobile Internet revolution, <a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/10/mobile-broadband-a-prediction-follow-up.html">which I wrote about in October of 2009</a>,&#0160;is already transforming the way consumers in developed markets access the Internet.&#0160; The bigger disruption is the entry of 1 billion new Internet users from emerging economies.&#0160; While many of these&#0160;people have relatively little education compared to Western Internet users, as the West&#0160;shrinks as a fraction of total Internet mindshare, many Western&#0160;cultural quirks that are seen as normal (such as institutionalized misandry sustained by an ever-expanding state) might be seen for the&#0160;aberrations that they are.&#0160; Thomas Friedman&#39;s concept of the world being &#39;flat&#39; has not even begun to fully manifest.&#0160;</p>
<p>7) <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/02/a_future_timeli.html">The energy sector is in the midst of multiple disruptions</a>, which will introduce competition between sectors that were previously unrelated.&#0160; Electrical vehicles displace oil consumption with electricity, even while the electricity itself starts to be generated through nuclear, <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2009/02/solar-powers-next-5-gamechanging-technologies.html">solar</a>, and wind.&#0160; The electrical economy will be further transformed by revolutions in <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/09/the_imminent_re.html">lighting</a> and <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2008/03/the-future-of-b.html">batteries</a>.&#0160; Cellulostic ethanol will arrive in 2012, and further replace billions of gallons of&#0160;gasoline.&#0160; <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/10/why-i-want-oil-.html">I wrote in October 2007 why I want oil to surpass $120/barrel and stay there</a> (it subsequently was above that level for a mere 6-week period in 2008).&#0160; This leads to why <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/03/rebuttal-to-a-f.html">I claim that &#39;Peak Oil&#39;, far from being fatal for civilization, will actually be a topic few people even mention in 2020</a>.&#0160; The creative destruction in energy will extend to the geopolitical landscape, where we will see many petrotyrannies much weaker in 2020 than they are today.&#0160;</p>
<p>8) Despite the efforts of Democrats to create a system unfavorable to&#0160;advancement in healthcare and biotechnology, <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/05/ten-biotechnolo.html">innovation continues on several fronts</a> (partly due to Asian nations compensating for US shortfalls).&#0160; One disruption is robotic surgery, where incisions can be narrow&#0160;instead of the customary practice of making incisions large enough for the surgeon&#39;s hands, which in turn often necessitates sawing open the sternum, pelvis, etc.&#0160; Intuitive Surgical is a company that already has a market cap of $14 Billion.&#0160;</p>
<p>The biggest disruption, however, is that the globalization of technology is enabling <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/08/more-on-the-economics-of-medical-tourism.html">medical tourism</a>.&#0160;&#0160;In the US, about twice as much is spent on healthcare as in other OECD countries.&#0160; If manufacturing and software work can be offshored, so can many aspects of healthcare, which is much more expensive than manufacturing or software engineering ever became in the US.&#0160; This will correct inflated salaries in the healthcare sector, return the savings to consumers, and force innovations and systemic improvements in all OECD countries.&#0160;</p>
<p><a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20133f1a4c7e1970b-pi" style="float: right;"><img alt="Genome" border="0" class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20133f1a4c7e1970b " src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20133f1a4c7e1970b-800wi" style="margin: 0px 0px 5px 5px;" title="Genome" /></a> 9) By all accounts, the cost of genome sequencing has plunged by a factor of 10,000 in just the last 4 years (it is less clear how this was accomplished, and whether the next 4 years will see a comparable drop).&#0160; I tend to be skeptical about such eye-popping numbers, because if something became so much cheaper so quickly, yet it still didn&#39;t sweep over the world, then maybe it was not so valuable after all.&#0160;</p>
<p>But it is also possible that while the raw data is now available cheaply, there is not yet enough of a community that instructs people how to use their data.&#0160; As more people sequence their genomes, networks of common genetic patterns will form, and health information will be shared.&#0160; Medicine will take on a Web 2.0 flavor, and physicians that realize they need to practice medicine as an information technology will thrive, while those who still adhere to the paternalistic paradigm will be left behind.&#0160; The standard medical diagnosis will be for a user to ask questions to others with the same genotype, and receive answers from multiple laypeople, and then, if necessary, take the information to a genome-savvy doctor.&#0160; <a href="http://www.economist.com/node/16349358?story_id=16349358">The Economist has a special report on the implications of inexpensive genome sequencing.</a>&#0160;</p>
<p>10) Social media such as Facebook, Twitter, etc. are mostly inundated with the trivialities of young people, or of older people who never matured, who think they have an audience far larger than it is.&#0160; However, these mediums have been used to horizontally organize interest groups and movements for political change that know no distance barriers or boundaries.&#0160;</p>
<p>Blogs have shattered the hold that traditional media had on the release of information and opinions, and the revenues of newspapers, magazines, and network television have tumbled.&#0160; The Tea Party movement in the US was started by a very small number of people, but&#0160;has surged with a momentum that has reshaped the American landscape in just one year, and, irony of ironies, the Tea Party is spreading to overtaxed Britain.&#0160; The next Iranian revolution will not only use Twitter and YouTube, but will have millions of collaborators outside of Iran, operating out of their own homes.&#0160;</p>
<p>11) The financial services industry currently charges $100 Billion in fees for the $3.3 Trillion in annual credit/debit card transactions that take place in the US alone.&#0160; <a href="http://www.wired.com/magazine/18-03">This was the cover story of Wired Magazine for March 2010</a>, and was a structure established in an era when computing power needed to process transactions was expensive.&#0160; Today, several startups are seeking to change the way money is transacted to eliminate this cut that incumbent companies take.&#0160; Major financial services companies will see shrinkages in revenue, and will have to innovate and create new value-added services, or accept a diminishment.&#0160;</p>
<p>Aside from this effectively being a sizable &#39;tax cut&#39; for the economy, this is particularly valuable as a complement to mobile Internet penetration in poorer regions, as the capacity to conduct web micro-transactions without fees will be an essential element of human development.&#0160; The highly successful concept of micro-finance will be augmented when transaction fees that consumed a high percentage of these sub-$10 transactions are minimized.&#0160;</p>
<p>12) 3-D Printing will soon be&#0160;accessible to small businesses and households.&#0160; This transforms everything from commodity consumer goods to the construction of buildings.&#0160; An individual could download a design and print it at home, rather than be restricted to only those products that can be mass produced.&#0160; <a href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/09/can-buildings-be-printed.html">It is quite possible that by 2025, construction of basic structures takes less than one-tenth the time that it does today</a>, which, of course, will deflate the value of all existing buildings in the world at that time.&#0160;</p>
<p>So we see&#0160;there are at least&#0160;12 ways in which our daily lives will shift considerably&#0160;in just&#0160;the next few years.&#0160; The typical process of creative destruction results in X wealth being destroyed, and 2X wealth being created instead, but by different people.&#0160; For each of the 12 disruptions listed, &#39;X&#39; might be as much a $1 Trillion.&#0160; As a result, the US economy might be mired in a&#0160;long-term situation where vanishing industries force many&#0160;laid off workers&#0160;to start in new industries&#0160;at the entry level,&#0160;for half of their previous compensation, even as new fortunes created by the new industries cause net wealth increases.&#0160; The US could see a continuation of high unemployment combined with high productivity gains and corporate earnings growth for several years to come.&#0160;&#0160;This would be the &#39;new normal&#39;.&#0160;</p>
<p>Income diversification is the golden rule of the early 21st&#0160;century.&#0160; Those that fail to create and maintain multiple streams of income are imperiling themselves.&#0160; The hottest career one can embark on, which will never be obsolete,&#0160;is that of the serial entrepreneur.&#0160;</p>
-----

TITLE: The Publishing Disruption
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: the-publishing-disruption
CATEGORY: Computing
CATEGORY: Consumer Technology
CATEGORY: Technological Breakthroughs

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2009/12/the-publishing-disruption.html 
DATE: 12/13/2009 10:53:00 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">What a unique thing a book is.&nbsp; Made from a tree, it has a hundred or more flexible pages that contain written text, enabling the book to contain a large sum of information in a very small volume.&nbsp; Before paper, clay tablets, sheepskin parchment, and papyrus were all used to store information with far less efficiency.&nbsp; Paper itself was once so rare and valuable that the Emperor of China had guards stationed around his paper posessions.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Before the invention of the printing press, books were written by hand, and few outside of monastaries knew how to read.&nbsp; There were only a few thousand books in all of Europe in the 14th century.&nbsp; Charlemagne himself took great effort to learn how to read, but never managed to learn how to write, which still put him ahead of most kings of the time, who were generally illiterate.&nbsp; </P>
<P>But with the invention of the printing press by Johannes Gutenberg in the mid-15th century, it became possible to make multiple copies of the same book, and before long, the number of books in Europe increased from thousands to millions.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Fast forward to the early 21st century, and books are still printed by the millions.&nbsp; Longtime readers of&nbsp;Techsector know that I initially had written a book (2001-02), and sought to have it published the old-fashioned way.&nbsp; However, the publishing industry, and literary agents, were astonishingly low-tech.&nbsp; They did not use email, and required queries to be submitted via regular mail, with a self-addressed, stamped envelope included.&nbsp; So I had to pay postage in both directions, and wait several days for a round trip to hear their response.&nbsp; And this was just the literary agents.&nbsp; The actual publishing house, if they decide to accept your book, would still take 12 months to produce and distribute the book even after the manuscript was complete.&nbsp; Even then, royalties would be 10-15% of the retail price.&nbsp; This prospect did not seem compelling to me, and I chose to parse my book into this blog you see before&nbsp;you.&nbsp; </P>
<P>The refusal by the publishing industry to use email and other productivity-enhancing technologies as recently as 2003 kept their wages low.&nbsp; Editors always moaned that they worked 60 hours a week just to make $50,000 a year, the same as they made in 1970.&nbsp; My answer to them is that they have no basis to expect wage increases without increasing their productivity through technology.&nbsp; </P>
<P>In the meantime, self-publishing technologies emerged to bypass the traditional publishers' role as arbitrers of what can become a book and what cannot.&nbsp; From Lulu to iUniverse to BookSmart, any individual can produce a book, with copies that can be printed on demand.&nbsp;&nbsp;Instances where an individual is seeking to go it alone without being saddled with a huge upfront inventory production and storage burden, or is otherwise marketing to only a tiny audience, have flourished.&nbsp; But print-on-demand is not the true disruption - that was yet to come.&nbsp; </P>
<P><A class=image href="http://www.typepad.com/wiki/File:Kindle_2_-_Front.jpg"></A><A style="FLOAT: right" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a74a54cc970b-pi"><img  class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20120a74a54cc970b " title=Kindle style="MARGIN: 0px 0px 5px 5px" alt=Kindle src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a74a54cc970b-800wi" border=0 /></A> The Amazon Kindle launched in late 2007 at the high price of $400.&nbsp; Within 2 years, <A href="http://www.economist.com/sciencetechnology/tq/displaystory.cfm?story_id=15048695">a substantially more advanced Kindle 2 was available for a much lower price of&nbsp;$260, alongside competing readers from several other companies</A>.&nbsp; Many&nbsp;people feel that the appeal of holding a physical book in our hands cannot be replaced by a display screen, and take a cavalier attitude towards dismissing e-readers.&nbsp; The tune changes upon learning that the price of a book on an e-reader is just a third of what the paper form at a brick-and-mortar bookstore, with sales tax, would cost.&nbsp; Market research firm iSuppli estimates that 5 million readers have been sold in 2009, and another 12 million will sell in 2010.&nbsp; Amazon estimates that over one-third of its book sales are now through the kindle, greatly displacing sales of paper books.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Imagine what happens when the Kindle and other e-readers cost only $100.&nbsp; Brick and mortar bookstores will consolidate to fewer premises, extract profits mainly from picture-heavy books and magazines, and&nbsp;step up their positioning&nbsp;as&nbsp;literary coffeehouses.&nbsp; Many employees and affiliates of the publishing industry will see their functions eliminated as part of the productivity gains.&nbsp; College students forced to pay $100 for a textbook produced in small quantities will now pay only $20 for an e-reader version.&nbsp; But even this is not the ultimate endgame of disruption.&nbsp; </P>
<P><A style="FLOAT: right" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a74a56b3970b-pi"><img  class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20120a74a56b3970b " title="Intel Reader" style="MARGIN: 0px 0px 5px 5px; WIDTH: 286px; HEIGHT: 349px" height=375 alt="Intel Reader" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a74a56b3970b-800wi" width=286 border=0 /></A> <A style="FLOAT: right" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a74a562d970b-pi"></A><A href="http://www.businessweek.com/magazine/content/09_50/b4159080740036.htm?chan=technology_tech+maven+page+-+new_digital+entertainment">Intel now has a reader for the visually impaired that scans text from paper books, and reads them in an acceptable audio voice</A>.&nbsp; It is reported that with practice, an audio rate of 250 words per minute can be coherent.&nbsp; While the reader costs $1500, and requires a user to turn pages manually, it is a matter of time before not only the reader's price drops,&nbsp;and more and more books are available as text files similar to those&nbsp;contained in&nbsp;e-readers like the Kindle.&nbsp; There are already books available as free downloads of text files under the ironically named Project Gutenberg.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Therein lies the crescendo of disruption.&nbsp; The Intel Reader is a $1500 device for the visually impaired, but will soon evolve into a technology that interfaces with Kindle-type e-readers and chatters off e-books at 250 words/minute, from the full e-book library that is vastly larger than any traditional collection of audiobooks.&nbsp; A 90,000-word novel could be recited in&nbsp;just 6&nbsp;hours, enabling a user to imbibe the whole&nbsp;book during a single coast-to-coast flight, even if the lights are dimmed.&nbsp; People could&nbsp;further choose to preserve their vision at home, devouring book after book with the lights out.&nbsp; As the technology advances further, the speech technology will allow the user to select a voice of his choosing to be read to in, perhaps even his own voice.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Thus, without many people even noticing the murmurs, we can&nbsp;predict that the next&nbsp;3 years will see the biggest&nbsp;transformation in book production and consumption since the days of Johannes Gutenberg.&nbsp; That is a true demonstration of both the <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/12/are_you_acceler.html">Accelerating Rate of Change</A> and <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2009/04/the-impact-of-computing-78-more-per-year-v20.html">The Impact of Computing</A>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</P>
-----

TITLE: Energy vs. Financials, FINAL RESULTS
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: energy-vs-financials-final-results
CATEGORY: Energy
CATEGORY: Stock Picks

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2009/11/energy-vs-financials-final-results.html 
DATE: 11/15/2009 09:18:10 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Many of you may be familiar with this sectoral strategy that I have presented, which was due to the unusually wide&nbsp;extreme to which these two sectors had diverged from each other as of April 22, 2008. To review :</P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/04/energy-vs-finan.html">On April 22, 2008, I decided to go short on Energy (XLE) and long on Financials (XLF).</A></P>
<P><A href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/05/energy-vs-financials-results.html">Then, on May 20, 2009, I decided to cover the Energy short, and use the proceeds to double down on Financials.&nbsp; Up till that point, the trade had earned a loss of -5.36%, vs. a loss of -32.20% for the S&P500</A>.</P>
<P>Now, it is time to sell the Financials position, and assess the final performance over the entire 18-month period, against the S&P 500.</P>
<P><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a685ea19970b-pi"><img  class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20120a685ea19970b image-full " title=Enerfin alt=Enerfin src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a685ea19970b-800wi" border=0 /></A> </P>
<P>The purple line indicates the May 20, 2009 transition from being short&nbsp;XLE to covering that short and using the full proceeds to double down on XLF.&nbsp; Note that the short of XLE was profitable, so that the amount that was redeployed to XLF was more than the existing value of the XLF position.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Therefore, the final results are (with all dividends reinvested) :</P>
<P><A style="DISPLAY: inline" onclick="window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e201287587db01970c-popup"><img  class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e201287587db01970c " title=Enerfin2 alt=Enerfin2 src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e201287587db01970c-800wi" border=0 /></A>&nbsp;<br>This strategy yielded a&nbsp;gain of <span style="COLOR: #007f40; FONT-FAMILY: ">21.92%</span> vs. a loss of <span style="COLOR: #ff4040; FONT-FAMILY: ">-17.19%</span> for the S&P500.&nbsp; This is a huge gap of almost 40 points, and means that $10,000 deployed to this strategy would have yielded $12,192, vs. just $8,281 if placed in the S&P500 over this period.&nbsp; Also note how the gap widened from what it was on May 20, 2009.&nbsp; </P>
<P>This continues <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2009/01/the-futurists-stock-portfolio-for-2008-results.html">our track record here at&nbsp;TechSector of&nbsp;collectively beating the market by a wide margin</A>, with portfolios that beat the market greatly exceeding the deficit of those that do not.&nbsp;&nbsp;Of course, these trades are for entertainment purposes only, and should not be taken as professional advice.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Related :</P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/10/a-history-of-stock-market-bottoms.html">A History of Stock Market Bottoms</A>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</P>

TITLE: A SmartGrid Primer
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: a-smartgrid-primer
CATEGORY: Consumer Technology
CATEGORY: Energy

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2009/11/a-smartgrid-primer.html 
DATE: 11/10/2009 11:26:00 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>The SmartGrid is the third major area of growth for the networking industry in the next decade, the other two being the previously discussed <a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/08/video-conferencing-a-cascade-of-disruptions.html">Telepresence</a> and <a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/10/mobile-broadband-a-prediction-follow-up.html">Mobile Internet</a> subsectors.&#0160; Like the other two sectors, the SmartGrid will disrupt many existing industries and infrastructures that have been undisturbed for several decades.&#0160; </p>
<p>Most of the world operates on an analog power grid built in the 19th and 20th centuries.&#0160; In the early 21st century, the advances in information technology can enable a comprehensive revamp of the existing electrical infrastructure.&#0160;&#0160;A slew of products are about to be released and deployed into the market that&#0160;can increase electricity supply by 20% or more, match revenue and costs more closely, reduce capital expenditures on generators and transmission lines/poles, and manage the demand differences between mid-day and night.&#0160; </p>
<p>Factors that will greatly change the daily cycle of electricity demand are solar&#0160;power being sent into the grid during the daytime, while millions of expected plug-in hybrid and fully-electric vehicles will be withdrawing power overnight.&#0160; The two key areas of growth are smart meter installation, and tranmission infrastructure upgrades.&#0160; </p>
<p>A few fast facts :</p>
<p>1) $20B in government injections will be devoted to SmartGrid upgrades from 2009-2014, including over $4.5B in the US.</p>
<p>2) A full upgrade will cost $200-$300 per endpoint.&#0160; </p>
<p>3) Companies that stand to benefit over the next 5 years include ESCO Technologies (ESCO), Itron (ITRI), Comverge (COMV), Silver Springs Networks, and eMeter.&#0160; </p>
<p>4) All backbone networking technologies like Powerline, WiMax, and fiber optics will benefit from the large amount of two-way data between the home and the utility.&#0160; </p>
<p>The arrival of the technology to form an entire SmartGrid ecosystem is timely for emerging countries, most notably India and China, in which many villages were not wired for electricity at all (40% of India&#39;s population still does not have electricity).&#0160; They can leapfrog directly to the SmartGrid, even for people receiving electricity for the first time.&#0160; </p>
<p>Of course, the old electrical grid will now be infused with devices governed by the <a href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2009/04/the-impact-of-computing-78-more-each-year.html">Impact of Computing</a>, which means that a household smart meters and control monitors&#0160;will drop in cost annually, and necessitate upgrades every few years.&#0160; Count this as one or more computing nodes entering into the average home.&#0160; </p>
<p><a href="http://www.businessweek.com/technology/content/oct2009/tc2009105_096734.htm">Businessweek has a comprehensive article on the SmartGrid, with a slideshow of technologies.&#0160; </a></p>
-----

TITLE: The Next Two US Recessions
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: the-next-two-us-recessions
CATEGORY: Economics

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2009/11/the-next-two-us-recessions.html 
DATE: 11/04/2009 02:48:22 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px;">Here at TechSector, we maintain a track record of predicting bubbles, busts, and recessions long before they happen.&nbsp; For example, <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/04/the_housing_bub.html">the housing bubble was identified in April 2006</A>, back when a person could be socially excommunicated for claiming that houses may not rise in value forever.&nbsp; After that, <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/02/recession-began.html">I have identified when the current recession started, months before most economists</A>, and have even <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2009/04/the-form-of-economic-recovery.html">predicted when the present recession will end, and at what level job losses would end at</A>.&nbsp; This track record will now lead me to set my sights on the next two troubles on the horizon, which will be the causes of the next two potential recessions.&nbsp;</P>
<P>1)<strong> 2011 :</strong> The tax cuts enacted by President Bush are set to expire at the end of 2010, returning tax brackets to what they were in 2000.&nbsp; Most middle class brackets will rise by 3%, and the top bracket will rise 4.6% from 35% to 39.6%.&nbsp; This is effectively a tax increase that will be upon us in 14 months.&nbsp; At the same time, the Fed Funds rate is at a record low near 0%, and has been for several months.&nbsp; This low interest rate has ended the current recession, but virtually guarantees future inflation.&nbsp; As the Federal Reserve is forced to raise interest rates, liquidity contracts again, the housing prices continue on the correction that was not allowed to complete itself in 2009.&nbsp; A mere rise in the rate back up to 3% could push housing prices down another leg, battering household wealth yet again, and driving yet more people into negative net worth.&nbsp; <strong>The housing correction is not fully complete until we have sustained a Fed Funds rate over 3% for at least a year.</strong>&nbsp; The timing of this could combine with the tax increase, which would&nbsp;create a joint burden too heavy for the economy to bear, causing a new recession in 2011.&nbsp; </P>
<P>This situation could be avoided easily,&nbsp;by reducing the budget deficit through the quaint notion of spending cuts instead of tax increases that&nbsp;stifle incentives and encumber small businesses.&nbsp; However, barring a seismic shift in the 2010 congressional elections that dispose of many Democrats and replace them with fiscally conservative Republicans (themselves an endangered group within the Republican Party), I do not see the government taking prudent preventive action.&nbsp; </P>
<P>2)&nbsp;<strong>~2017</strong> : For all the uninformed talk about a 'weak dollar', the damage of this will affect nations that export to the US more than the US itself. &nbsp;However, when&nbsp;the PPP per capita GDP of China begins to exceed the world average (by about 2017), then Chinese currency will have to rise to achieve convergence with nominal GDP, effectively adding several trillion dollars to nominal World GDP.&nbsp; This will lead to massive tectonic shifts in the global economy, none of which are destructive, but will result in confusion, for which the immediate reaction will be a US and EU recession (even amidst a rise in World GDP) until all of the following effects are sorted out :</P>
<P>a) The US will see a bout of inflation as prices of Chinese&nbsp;imports rise.&nbsp; At the same time, US exports will surge.</P>
<P>b) Oil prices will spike above $120 as Chinese purchasing power of oil rises, effectively creating greater demand.&nbsp; This will cause short-term pain, <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/10/why-i-want-oil-.html">followed by longer-term good as described here</A>.&nbsp;&nbsp;Part of the good from an oil spike will be the collapse of many tyrannical petro-regimes, due to burning the candle at both ends, as detailed in the link.</P>
<P>c) Many of the developing countries that neighbor China&nbsp;(which are populated by an additional 2 billion people) will experience the gravitational pull of China's now huge economy, and see a forced currency appreciation long before they are ready.&nbsp;&nbsp;This will cause an unexpected set of changes in India, Pakistan, Bangladesh, VietNam, the Philipines, and Indonesia (these 6 countries containing 2 billion people) as a massive adjustment process will have to occur in a very short time, toppling many industries and creating new ones within these countries.&nbsp; </P>
<P>d) After everything is sorted out, the US and EU will be significantly smaller percentages of World GDP, but the US would see higher GDP growth rates due to a near-elimination of the trade-deficit.&nbsp; Asia, as a region, would have a much larger economy than the EU or North America.&nbsp; </P>
<P>So these are the two possible recessions that the US faces, the first in 2011 and the second in the latter half of the next decade.&nbsp; Prudent fiscal management could&nbsp;sidestep the first, while the second is an inevitable byproduct of the adjustments borne of poverty reduction.&nbsp; </P>
<P>In any event, investment opportunities, and, more importantly, bullet-dodging opportunities abound.&nbsp; In the immediate term, however, if you are considering buying a home in an expensive US area such as New York or California, <span style="text-decoration: underline">do not buy one</span>.&nbsp; &nbsp;</P>
<P>Related :</P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/04/the_housing_bub.html">The Housing Bubble - 20-Year Gains May Never be Repeated</A></P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/09/a-future-timeline-for-economics.html">A Future Timeline for Economics</A></P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/10/why-i-want-oil-.html">Why I Want Oil to Hit $120/Barrel</A><br><br></P>

TITLE: Mobile Broadband : A Prediction Follow Up
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: mobile-broadband-a-prediction-follow-up
CATEGORY: Internet

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2009/10/mobile-broadband-a-prediction-follow-up.html 
DATE: 10/09/2009 08:48:13 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Some of you may recall that over three and a half years ago, <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/02/the_end_of_rabb.html">on February 4, 2006, I predicted that by 2013, at least 900 million people in emerging nations, 80% of whom had no Internet connection in 2006,&nbsp;would have access to a wireless broadband connection through their cellphones</A>.&nbsp; That seemed like a bold prediction at the time.</P>
<P><A style="FLOAT: right" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a5bcef78970b-pi"><img  class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20120a5bcef78970b " title=Mobile style="MARGIN: 0px 0px 5px 5px" alt=Mobile src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a5bcef78970b-800wi" border=0 /></A> But in the Economist, there is a special report on mobile phones in the developing world, and this chart depicts the progress towards my prediction quite nicely.&nbsp; Mobile broadband subscribers will go from nearly zero in early 2006 when the prediction was first made, to 1.4 billion by 2013 (of which 900 million can safely be assumed to be in&nbsp;emerging nations).&nbsp; </P>
<P>It is often said that no other invention has done more for so many people so quickly than the mobile phone, given the large number of people who did not have even a landline phone prior to getting a mobile phone.&nbsp; However, the inital deployment of rudimentary mobile phones was just the beginning.&nbsp; As 3G broadband at speeds greater than 1 mbps spread to a billion people with no prior Internet access, the entire nature of their existence is transformed.&nbsp; As per this second chart from the Economist report, the GDP boost from broadband Internet penetration is far higher than the already-impressive boost we have seen from simple mobile access, and we can thus expect another, stronger wave of human advancement as mobile broadband diffuses.&nbsp;<A style="FLOAT: right" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a62724ed970c-pi"><img  class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00d83452455969e20120a62724ed970c " title=Mobile2 style="MARGIN: 0px 0px 5px 5px" alt=Mobile2 src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20120a62724ed970c-800wi" border=0 /></A> Simultaneously, the entire nature of the Internet is also transformed.&nbsp; Think of the massive developmental catalyst such a rapid technological diffusion would be.&nbsp; Child literacy would rise as&nbsp;the educational materials&nbsp;of the full Internet will&nbsp;be available in locations where no libraries exist, making near-universal child literacy a reality within a decade.&nbsp; Agricultural and fishery supply chains will shorten tremendously.&nbsp; Disaster&nbsp;relief will become far easier, as will the apprehension of criminals.&nbsp; The upliftment&nbsp;that once appeared to be a process of decades will now happen in mere years.&nbsp; &nbsp; </P>
<P>We can thus proceed to the next prediction, which is that <strong>by 2020, 4 billion people will have 4G wireless broadband access on their handheld mobile phone, at speeds exceeding 100 Mbps.</strong>&nbsp; In other words, a landline speed that even wealthy Americans could not have in 2005 will be available wirelessly to billions of the very poorest people&nbsp;just 15 years later, in 2020.&nbsp; Imagine that.&nbsp; </P>
