AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: Why Government is Set to Extinguish Silicon Valley
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: why-government-is-set-to-extinguish-silicon-valley
CATEGORY: Consumer Technology
CATEGORY: Economics

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2009/01/why-government-is-set-to-extinguish-silicon-valley.html 
DATE: 01/25/2009 02:47:52 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">
<blockquote dir=ltr style="MARGIN-RIGHT: 0px">
<P><em><span style="COLOR: #0000bf; FONT-FAMILY: Trebuchet MS">Throughout history, poverty is the normal condition of man. Advances which permit this norm to be exceeded — here and there, now and then — are the work of an extremely small minority, frequently despised, often condemned, and almost always opposed by all right-thinking people. Whenever this tiny minority is kept from creating, or (as sometimes happens) is driven out of a society, the people then slip back into abject poverty.</span></em></P>
<blockquote dir=ltr style="MARGIN-RIGHT: 0px">
<P style="TEXT-ALIGN: right">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;- Robert Heinlein</P></blockquote></blockquote></span>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">The secret sauce of Silicon Valley is the tradition of leaving established companies to start or join new ones, secure funding from venture capitalists, build the company to a suitable size, and then either float or sell the company for a windfall to the founders and early employees.&nbsp; The incentive to continue this practice is the engine that keeps the fire of human technological innovation alive.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Silicon Valley's unique ecosystem has so far been nearly impossible to eclipse.&nbsp; The combination of research universities, the best and brightest immigrants from India and China, a culture of entrepreneurship, and a nearly perfect climate has kept the competitors to Silicon Valley at bay.&nbsp; In the 1990s, the prevalent belief was that the high cost of living in Silicon Valley would enable Austin, Dallas, Seattle, and&nbsp;Phoenix to attract technology workers and cultivate their own tech sectors.&nbsp; This did not happen, as the Silicon Valley ecosystem just had too strong of a gravitational pull.&nbsp; </P>
<P>This, however, should not be an excuse for complacency, or&nbsp;a belief that Silicon Valley is a bottomless supply of tax revenue.&nbsp; There are four&nbsp;steps that would make Silicon Valley prohibitively inhospitable to the formation of new ventures.&nbsp;The first two of these&nbsp;four steps have already happened, and the final two are set to happen, barring direct intervention.&nbsp; </P>
<P><strong>1) Sarbanes-Oxley :</strong> This attempt to reduce the risk of another Enron-style fraud has inflicted a cost on the US economy greater than 100 Enron collapses.&nbsp; In Silicon Valley, the crushing costs of Sarbanes-Oxley compliance (up to $3M a year)&nbsp;have dried up IPOs to a trickle, as the prospect of spending money of compliance that could otherwise be spent on R&amp;D is unappealing.&nbsp; IPOs are less frequent than they were even in the early 1990s, before the bubble, and start-ups can only hope to be acquired by a larger&nbsp;company.&nbsp; In the last 8 years, only two IPOs were large enough to be considered 'blockbuster' : Google and VMWare.&nbsp; This crushes the incentive to leave stable jobs to go work at a new venture.&nbsp; </P>
<P><strong>2) Immigration Torture :</strong> Any list of the most successful people in the history of Silicon Valley will quickly reveal that at least one third of them were born outside of the US.&nbsp; In response, America has chosen to make it much harder for more such people to come here.&nbsp; </P>
<P>While politicians pander to <em>illegal</em> immigrants with&nbsp;minimal education, they somehow refuse to make immigration easier for <em>legal</em>, highly-skilled immigrants who start new ventures in America.&nbsp; This is significant given the fact that about half of Silicon Valley's skilled workforce is Indian or&nbsp;Chinese.&nbsp; They could return to their home countries easily if it becomes too hard to stay.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Under current procedures, an engineer from India or China has to be on an H1-B visa for 6 years before he can get a greencard.&nbsp; If he changes employers during that period, he has to start the clock again.&nbsp; The immigrant's spouse cannot work during this period.&nbsp; Even after the greencard, it takes 5 more years to become a US citizen.&nbsp; More and more of the best and brightest are deciding that this&nbsp;11-year limbo is not worth it, and return to their home countries (eventually starting companies there rather than in Silicon Valley).&nbsp;&nbsp;In the&nbsp;1990s,&nbsp;Americans had not even heard of Bangalore&nbsp;or Suzhou.&nbsp; </P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/05/further_thought.html">I have written a detailed solution to this problem over here.</A>&nbsp; </P>
<P>___________________________________________________________________________</P>
<P>If these two factors weren't bad enough, two more negatives are about to be piled on.&nbsp; </P>
<P><strong>3) California State Income Taxes are Set to Rise :</strong> The budget shortfalls and underfunded pensions in California are a ticking time bomb.&nbsp; CalPERS, which invests in many of the top venture capital funds that nurture the growth of start-ups in Silicon Valley, is in a shambolic state, and has to add $80 billion in assets just to meet present obligations.&nbsp; The top income bracket in California is already taxed at 9.3%, and this is set to rise.&nbsp; Sales taxes are also set to rise.&nbsp; Due to this horrendous mismanagement worthy of a banana republic, California will soon reach a tipping point where taxes are so high as to destroy California's private sector.&nbsp; It would, of course, be better to reduce CA state expenditures, but government officials have made it clear that raising taxes is their preferred course of action.&nbsp; </P>
<P><strong>4) Federal Income Taxes are Set to Rise :</strong> If the Bush tax cuts are allowed to expire, then from 2011 onwards, the top income bracket will be taxed at 39.6% rather than the current 35%.&nbsp; Here, too, the concept of reducing expenditures is not palatable to Washington decision-makers.&nbsp; While this does affect the entire US equally, when this is combined with the increase in California Sate tax, the combined marginal tax rate in California rises several percentage points, and possibly&nbsp;rises well above&nbsp;50%.</P>
<P>The danger here is that each of these factors by themselves are not life-threatening.&nbsp; But all four of them in cumulative combination are deadly.&nbsp; So on top of the difficulty of conducting an&nbsp;IPO, and the brain drain out of Silicon Valley back to Asia, if the financial windfall that a worker receives after his startup makes a successful exit is taxed at a grand total of 50-55%, fewer and fewer people will bother to toil away for years in a startup.&nbsp; As a result, fewer startups will form in Silicon Valley, and instead will form in Bangalore, Shanghai, and Taipei.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Furthermore, after these forces have been in effect for a few years a simple reversal of the higher tax rates, dysfunctional immigration policy, and Sarbanes Oxley will not simply restore Silicon Valley to its prior grandeur.&nbsp; The technology centers in Asia will have achieved critical mass by then, and Silicon Valley&nbsp;will have permanently lost its exclusivity.&nbsp; It would never recover the dominance it once had.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Silicon Valley will be reduced to a location that still hosts the headquarters of HP, Intel, Cisco, and Google, but 90% of the employees of these corporations will be overseas, and startups will be rare.&nbsp; Silicon Valley will effectively become like Cleveland or Pittsburgh, which even today host the headquarters of more than 20 Fortune 500 corporations each, but still have a lower population than they each had in 1960, and cannot attract new young people to come and live there.&nbsp; Cleveland and Pittsburgh are still functioning societies, of course, but their economic vibrancy is irretrievably dead.&nbsp; </P>
<P>This bleak outlook can certainly be reversed if prompt action is taken now.&nbsp; Sadly, the current path is one that is set to have a smothering effect on Silicon Valley.&nbsp; </P>
<P></P>
-----

TITLE: 2008 Technology Breakthrough Roundup
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: 2008-technology-breakthrough-roundup
CATEGORY: Science
CATEGORY: Technological Breakthroughs

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2009/01/2008-technology-breakthrough-roundup.html 
DATE: 01/03/2009 03:07:47 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Each year, I post a roundup of technology breakthroughs for that year from the MIT Technology Review, and I now present the 2008 edition.&nbsp; </P>
<P>2008 was a year of unusually low technological innovation.&nbsp; This is not merely the byproduct of the economic recession, as some forms of innovation actually quicken during a recession.&nbsp; Furthermore, the innovations from 2006 and 2007 (linked below) showed very little additional progress in 2008, except in the field of energy.&nbsp; This also supports my observation from February 2008 <A href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2008/02/is-technology-d.html">that technology diffusion is in an unexplained lull</A>.&nbsp; </P>
<P>The innovations in 2008 are categorized below :&nbsp; </P>
<P><A href="http://www.technologyreview.com/computing/21895/?a=f">The Year in Computing</A></P>
<P><A href="http://www.technologyreview.com/computing/21897/?a=f">The Year in Robotics</A></P>
<P><A href="http://www.technologyreview.com/biomedicine/21894/">The Year in Biomedicine</A></P>
<P><A href="http://www.technologyreview.com/computing/21900/?a=f">The Year in Materials</A></P>
<P>What is conspicuously absent is any article titled 'The Year in Nanotechnology'.&nbsp; Both 2006 and 2007 had such articles, but the absence of a 2008 version speaks volumes about how little innovation took place in 2008.&nbsp; The entire field on nanotechnology was lukewarm.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Most of the innovations in the articles above are in the laboratory phase, which means that about half will never progress enough to make it to market, and those that do will take 5 to 15 years to directly affect the lives of average people (remember that the laboratory-to-market transition period itself continues to shorten in most fields).&nbsp; </P>
<P>Furthermore, <A href="http://online.wsj.com/article/SB122227003788371453.html?mod=article-outset-box">The Wall Street Journal has its own innovation awards for 2008</A>, but this merely confirms that 2008 was a poor year for innovation.&nbsp; For example, the Tata Nano is chosen in the WSJ article, yet it is not available to consumers until mid-2009.&nbsp; Let's hope 2009 has more genuine innovations.&nbsp; </P>

<P>Into the future we continue, where 2009 awaits....</P>
<P>Related :</P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/01/2007-technology.html">2007 Technology Breakthrough Roundup</A></P>
<P><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/12/2006_technology.html">2006 Technology Breakthrough Roundup</A></P>

TITLE: It's Official : US Recession Began in December 2007
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: its-official-us-recession-began-in-december-2007
CATEGORY: Economics

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/12/its-official-us-recession-began-in-december-2007.html 
DATE: 12/02/2008 01:09:16 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">The Nation Bureau of Economic Research (NBER) said that the US has been in recession since December 2007.&nbsp; <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/02/recession-began.html">Of course, that is precisely the time I had given over on The Futurist, except that my declaration was back in&nbsp;February 2008</A>.&nbsp; So I arrived at the same timing estimation, just&nbsp;10 months before the Federal Government, back when knowing this would actually have been useful.&nbsp; </P>
<P>The longest post-war recession in the US&nbsp;lasted 16 months, so if this recession lasts beyond April of 2009 (which it very well may), it would be the longest post-war recession the US has had.&nbsp; Of course, this recession was shallow for the first 10 months, and only turned sharply lower in October of 2008, so 'duration' is not the whole story.&nbsp; </P>
<P>At present, the consensus is that all of 2009 will effectively be recessionary, putting the recession at 24 months in total duration.&nbsp; Whether this occurs or that pessimism itself is over-shooting remains to be seen.&nbsp; </P>

AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: A History of Stock Market Bottoms
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: a-history-of-stock-market-bottoms
CATEGORY: Economics
CATEGORY: Stock Market Inflection Points
CATEGORY: Stock Picks

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/10/a-history-of-stock-market-bottoms.html 
DATE: 10/12/2008 05:39:00 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Recent market turmoil has many wondering when the freefall will cease, and whether we are on the brink of a new Great Depression, which is supposed to happen every 70-80 years according to Kondratieff Wave theory.&nbsp; I don't believe we are on the brink of a depression, <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/02/recession-began.html">even though the present recession&nbsp;is already in its 10th month</A>.&nbsp; But it would be instructive to compare the current situation with prior market corrections, and judge the present situation in a historical context.&nbsp; </P>
<P>We can first start with a chart of the S&amp;P500, from 1950 to today.&nbsp; We can see that the deepest deviations from the trendline appear to be in 1950, 1970, 1974, 1982, 1987, 1990, and 2002.&nbsp; We shall term these instances as historical 'bottoms' for the stock market.&nbsp; All but the 1987 bottom were in the midst of economic recessions.&nbsp; </P>
<P><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e201053583868e970c-pi"></A><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20105358386ba970c-pi"></A><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e2010535962f87970b-pi"><img  class="at-xid-6a00d83452455969e2010535962f87970b image-full " title=S&amp;P500 alt=S&amp;P500 src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e2010535962f87970b-800wi" border=0></A> &nbsp;&nbsp; </P>
<P>From this chart, we can see that the time period between bottoms can be irregular, with over a decade passing between them, in some cases.&nbsp; 1974 and 1982 appear to be the deepest corrections.&nbsp; These bottoms coincide with recessions, but interestingly do not coincide with other major crises.&nbsp; The Cuban Missile Crisis, Kennedy assassination, and 9/11/01 did not induce major market crashes beyond the first few days.&nbsp; Now, we can take the datapoints of each of these bottoms, and chart the exponential trendline that connects them.&nbsp; This is purely a chart of index valuation, with dividends not included.&nbsp; </P>
<P><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e2010535839c6a970c-pi"></A><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20105359d8998970c-pi"></A><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e2010535cc25ec970c-pi"><img  class="at-xid-6a00d83452455969e2010535cc25ec970c image-full " title=Bottoms alt=Bottoms src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e2010535cc25ec970c-800wi" border=0></A> &nbsp;&nbsp; </P>
<P>From this chart. we can see that the equivalent value of the S&amp;P500 in 2008, as designated by the red circle, would be around the 1000 level.&nbsp; As of October 10, 2008, the S&amp;P500 is at 899, or 10% below the level of the bottoms trendline.&nbsp; However, we can see that both the 1974 and 1982 bottoms are substantially below the trendline.&nbsp; </P>
<P>The S&amp;P500, since 1950, has delivered an 11.4% average return, with 7.7% of that in the form of a rise in the index itself, and 3.7% of the return being in the form of dividends.&nbsp;&nbsp;If the long-term underlying growth rate of the index is 7.7%, we can chart a 7.7% compounded projection trend from each of these bottoms as another method to compare them to an approximate 2008 equivalent.&nbsp; We shall start this chart from 1970.&nbsp; </P>
<P><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e20105357aeb88970b-pi"></A><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e2010535963293970b-pi"></A><A style="DISPLAY: inline" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e2010535cc2605970c-pi"><img  class="at-xid-6a00d83452455969e2010535cc2605970c image-full " title=7projS&amp;P500 alt=7projS&amp;P500 src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e2010535cc2605970c-800wi" border=0></A> &nbsp;&nbsp;</P>
<P>It is apparent that 4 of the 6 bottoms cluster around a 2008 projection of 1100-1200, but the two deepest bottoms of 1974 and 1982 project to a 2008 equivalent of only 700-750.&nbsp; These should be considered the two 'mega-bottoms' that happen&nbsp;a couple times&nbsp;per half-century, with the other 4 being only smaller bottoms that happen every 7-10 years, whenever there is a recession.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Since we are presently at 900 for the S&amp;P500, we are about half-way between a smaller bottom and a mega-bottom.&nbsp; Therefore, do not be surprised if the S&amp;P500 does, in fact, dip into the low 700s in 2009, merely to match&nbsp;this correction&nbsp;to 1974/1982 levels.&nbsp; This would be a further 20% correction from the&nbsp;900 close of October 10, 2008.&nbsp; It may&nbsp;not happen, but it certainly could in&nbsp;terms of historical precedent.&nbsp;&nbsp;This also means that the Dow Jones Industrial Average&nbsp;would&nbsp;simultaneously decline to as low&nbsp;as 6500.&nbsp; Indeed, there is no&nbsp;guarantee that it could not go even lower, but that would he historically unprecedented.&nbsp; Even&nbsp;the 1932 bottom in the Great Depression was not deeper than the 1974 and 1982 bottoms, by these measures.&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</P>
<P><strong>The Good News :</strong></P>
<P>If the thought of a further 20% decline in the S&amp;P500 or DJIA is depressing, also consider the following :</P>
<P>1) After both the 1974 and 1982 mega-bottoms, the&nbsp;stock market promptly returned at least&nbsp;60% in the next 9 months.&nbsp;&nbsp;This also happened after the 1932 bottom within the Great Depression.&nbsp; </P>
<P>2) Never forget about dividend reinvestment.&nbsp;&nbsp;Dividend yields are highest when the stock market is&nbsp;at the depths&nbsp;of a bottom, and reinvestment ensures&nbsp;that new shares are purchased at the lower prices.&nbsp; This enables the investor to enhance&nbsp;his&nbsp;returns when the recovery finally commences.&nbsp;&nbsp;Even in the 1970s, the major indices were stuck within a flat range for a decade, but dividend yields as high as 5% enabled total returns that were substantially better.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Considering&nbsp;points 1) and 2), make sure that you are in a position to capture the recovery,&nbsp;and are not forced to sell at the unfavorable&nbsp;prices of the bottom.&nbsp; This means that you must a) never hold any substantial margin debt, b)&nbsp;be positioned across a diversifed set of&nbsp;securities, preferably ETFs ahead of individual stocks, and c) watch as little financial news as possible, thereby reducing your chances of panic that could lead you to take ill-considered actions.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</P>
<P>Tremendous profits will be made by those who can steel themselves through this purging of the weak, and are subsequently prepared for the post-bottom recovery.&nbsp; Put daily volatility aside, and enjoy the historical times that we are experiencing first-hand.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Related :</P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/07/economic-growth.html">Economic Growth is Exponential and Accelerating</A></P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/04/the_housing_bub.html">The Housing Bubble - 20-year Gains May Never be Repeated</A></P>
<P>(<A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/10/a-history-of-stock-market-bottoms.html">crossposted on The Futurist</A>)</P>

AUTHOR: The Futurist

TITLE: Pre-Singularity Abundance Milestones
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: pre-singularity-abundance-milestones
CATEGORY: Accelerating Change
CATEGORY: Biotechnology
CATEGORY: Computing
CATEGORY: Consumer Technology
CATEGORY: The Singularity

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/09/pre-singularity-abundance-milestones.html 
DATE: 09/24/2008 10:50:12 AM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">I am of the belief that we will experience a Technological Singularity around 2050 or shortly thereafter. Many top futurists all arrive at prediction dates between 2045 and 2075. The bulk of Singularity debate revolves not so much around 'if' or even 'when', but rather 'what' the Singularity will appear like, and whether it will be positive or negative for humanity. </P>
<P>To be clear, some singularities have already happened.&nbsp; To non-human creatures, a technological singularity that overhauls their ecosystem already happened over the course of the 20th century.&nbsp; Domestic dogs and cats are immersed in a singularity where most of their surroundings surpass their comprehension.&nbsp; Even many humans have experienced a singularity -&nbsp;elderly people&nbsp;in poorer nations make no use of any of the major technologies of the last 20 years, except possibly the cellular phone.&nbsp;&nbsp;However, the Singularity that I am talking about <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/08/top-ten-transhu.html">has to be one that affects all humans</A>, and the entire global economy, rather that just humans that are marginal participants in the economy.&nbsp; By definition, the real Technological Singularity has to be a 'disruption in the fabric of humanity'.&nbsp; </P>
<P>In the period between 2008 and 2050, there are several milestones one can watch for in order to see if the path to a possibile Singularity is still being followed.&nbsp; Each of these signifies a previously scarce resource becoming almost infinitely abundant (much like paper today, which was a rare and precious&nbsp;treasure centuries ago), or a dramatic expansion in human experience (such as the telephone, airplane, and Internet have been) to the extent that it can even be called a <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/08/top-ten-transhu.html">transhuman experience</A>.&nbsp; The following are a random selection of milestones with their anticipated dates.&nbsp; </P>
<P><strong>Technological&nbsp;:</strong></P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/10/ciscos_hd_video.html">Hours spent in videoconferencing surpass hours spent in air travel/airports</A> : 2015</P>
<P>Video games with interactive, human-level AI : 2018</P>
<P>Semi-realistic fully immersive virtual reality : 2020</P>
<P>Over&nbsp;5 billion people connected to the Internet (mostly wirelessly) at speeds greater than 10 Mbps : 2022</P>
<P>Over 30 network-connected devices in the average household&nbsp;worldwide : 2025</P>
<P>1 TeraFLOPS of computing power costs $1 : 2026</P>
<P>1 TeraWatt of worldwide photovoltaic&nbsp;power capacity&nbsp;: 2027</P>
<P>1 Petabyte of storage costs&nbsp;$1 : 2028</P>
<P>1 Terabyte of RAM costs $1 : 2031</P>
<P>An artificial intelligence can pass the Turing Test : 2040</P>
<P><strong>Biological :</strong></P>
<P><A href="http://www.technologyreview.com/Biotech/21341/?a=f">Complete personal genome sequencing</A> costs $1000 : 2020</P>
<P>Cancer is no longer&nbsp;one of the top 5&nbsp;causes of death : 2025</P>
<P><A href="http://www.technologyreview.com/Biotech/21341/?a=f">Complete personal genome sequencing</A> costs $10 : 2030</P>
<P>Human life expectancy achieves <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/03/actuarial-escap.html">Actuarial Escape Velocity</A> for wealthy individuals : 50% chance by 2040</P>
<P><strong>Economic : </strong></P>
<P>Average US household net worth crosses $2 million in nominal dollars : 2024</P>
<P>90% of humans living in nations with a <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/11/new_un_human_de_1.html">UN Human Development Index</A> greater than 0.800 (the 2008 definition of a 'developed country', approximately that of the US in 1960) : 2025</P>
<P>10,000 billionaires worldwide (nominal dollars) : 2030</P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/07/economic-growth.html">World GDP per Capita crosses $50,000 in 2008 dollars</A> : 2045</P>
<P>_________________________________________________________________</P>
<P>Each of these milestones, while not causing a Singularity by themselves,&nbsp;increase the probability of a true Technological Singularity, with the event horizon pulled in closer to that date.&nbsp; Or, the path taken to each of these milestones may give rise to new questions and metrics altogether.&nbsp; We must watch for each of these events, and update our predictions for the 'when' and 'what' of the Singularity accordingly.&nbsp; </P>
<P>Related : <A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/08/top-ten-transhu.html">The Top 10 Transhumanist Technologies</A></P>

COMMENT:
AUTHOR: ces
EMAIL: cesium62@yahoo.com
IP: 65.57.245.11
URL: 
DATE: 12/01/2008 09:54:23 AM
"Interactive human-level AI in 2018" and "AI can pass the Turing test in 2040" are mutually contradictory.  If we have convincing human level AI in 2018, it isn't going to take 22 more years to prove that we have convincing human level AI.

So why are any of these statements and dates meaningful?  Did you just make up the dates?

You should pull together some of your technology estimates for storage costs, ram costs and cpu costs.  The "3M" machine (1 megahertz, 1 megabyte and 1 megapixel) was widely touted leading up to 1984; and this was a breakthrough combination of technology ushering in the GUI.  

One could have constructed a similar "3G" concept for 2004.  (1 Gigahertz cpu and 1 Gigabyte ram were reasonably available.  But my widescreen display only has 125megabits (32*2560*1600).  Gigabit local area networking was potentially available.  Presumably the 3G computer ushered in some user visible innovation -- probably the widespread internet; maybe video editing.

For 2024, the "3T" computer could well be defined:  1 Teraflops of cpu, 1 Terabyte of RAM, 1 Terabit/sec of networking, 10s to 100s of terabytes of mass storage, 10s of gigabits of display.  I'm not sure what the user experience breakthrough will be.  But this powerful system is not the AI system.

The AI system would show up around 2024 in a "3P" computer:  1 petaflops of cpu, 1 petabyte of ram, 1 petabit/sec of networking, 1 petabyte of mass storage, 1 terabit of display.

TITLE: Can Buildings be 'Printed'?
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: can-buildings-be-printed
CATEGORY: Accelerating Change
CATEGORY: Economics
CATEGORY: Technological Breakthroughs
CATEGORY: The Singularity

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/09/can-buildings-be-printed.html 
DATE: 09/02/2008 04:03:27 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px" MS? Trebuchet FONT-FAMILY: 14px; FONT-SIZE:>I have discussed the possibility of 3-D printing of solid objects before, </span><A href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2008/02/six-tantalizing.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px" MS? Trebuchet FONT-FAMILY: 14px; FONT-SIZE:>in this article where company #5, Desktop Factory, is detailed</span></span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px" MS? Trebuchet FONT-FAMILY: 14px; FONT-SIZE:>. &nbsp;However, the Desktop Factory product can only produce objects that have a maximum size of 5 X 5 X 5 inches, and it can only use one type of material.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px" MS? Trebuchet FONT-FAMILY: 14px; FONT-SIZE:>On the Next Big Future blog, </span><A href="http://nextbigfuture.com/2008/08/caterpillar-inc-funds-viterbi-print.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px" MS? Trebuchet FONT-FAMILY: 14px; FONT-SIZE:>the author quite frequently profiles a future product capable of 'printing' entire buildings</A>. This technology, known as <A href="http://www.contourcrafting.org/">'Contour Crafting'</A>, can supposedly construct buildings at greater than 10 times the speed, yet at just one-fifth the cost of traditional construction processes. It is claimed that the first commercial machines will be available in 2008 itself. </P>
<P><A onblur="try {parent.deselectBloggerImageGracefully();} catch(e) {}" href="http://bp0.blogger.com/_VyTCyizqrHs/SEjKVkpI5jI/AAAAAAAAApU/FJv8puXQY3w/s1600-h/printingbuildings.jpg"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><img  id=BLOGGER_PHOTO_ID_5208635441016268338 style="FLOAT: left; MARGIN: 0px 10px 10px 0px; CURSOR: hand" alt="" src="http://bp0.blogger.com/_VyTCyizqrHs/SEjKVkpI5jI/AAAAAAAAApU/FJv8puXQY3w/s400/printingbuildings.jpg" border=0></A></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px" MS? Trebuchet FONT-FAMILY: 14px; FONT-SIZE:>Despite my general optimism, this particular machine does not pass my 'too good to be true' test, at least before 2020. A machine that could construct homes and commercial buildings at such a speed and cost would cause an unprecedented economic disruption across the world. There would be a steep but brief depression, as existing real estate loses 90% or more of its value, followed by a huge boom as home ownership becomes affordable to several times as many people as today. I don't think that we are on the brink of such a revolution.</P>
<P>For me to be convinced, I would have to see :</P>
<P>1) Articles on this device in mainstream publications like The Economist, BusinessWeek, MIT Technology Review, or Popular Mechanics.</P>
<P>2) The ability to at least print simple constructs like concrete perimeter walls or sidewalks at a rate and cost several times superior to current methods. Only then can more complex structures be on the horizon.</P>
<P>I will revisit this technology if either of these two conditions is solidly met. </P>
<P>(<A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2008/09/can-buildings-be-printed.html">crossposted on The Futurist</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px; FONT-FAMILY: Trebuchet MS">)</span></span></P></span></span></span></span></span>

TITLE: A Future Timeline for Automobiles
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: a-future-timeline-for-automobiles

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/08/a-future-timeline-for-automobiles.html 
DATE: 08/01/2008 11:44:44 AM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">Many streams of <A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/12/are_you_acceler.html">accelerating technological change</A>, from <A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/energy/index.html">energy</A> to <A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/02/the_impact_of_c.html">The Impact of Computing</A>, will find themselves intersecting in one of the largest consumer product industries of all.&nbsp; Over 70 million automobiles were produced worldwide in 2007, with rapid market penetration underway in <A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2007/02/indiachina_grow.html">India and China</A>.&nbsp; Indisputably, cars greatly affect the lives of consumers, the economies of nations, and the market forces of technological change.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">I thus present a speculative timeline of technological and economic events that will happen for automobiles.&nbsp; This has numerous points of intersection with the <A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2007/02/a_future_timeli.html">Future Timeline for Energy</A>.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong><A onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=508,height=367,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2007/05/05/tesla_roadster.jpg"><img title=Tesla_roadster style="FLOAT: right; MARGIN: 0px 0px 5px 5px; WIDTH: 230px; HEIGHT: 144px" height=144 alt=Tesla_roadster src="https://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/images/2007/05/05/tesla_roadster.jpg" width=230 border=0></A>2008 :</strong> <A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/12/the_tesla_roads.html">The Tesla Roadster</A> emerges to not only bring Silicon Valley change agents together to sow the seeds of disruption in the automotive industry, but also to immediately transform the image of electrical vehicles from 'punishment cars' to status symbols of dramatic sex appeal.&nbsp; Even at the price of $92,000, demand outstrips supply by an impressive margin.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2009</strong> : The </span><A href="http://www.popularmechanics.com/blogs/automotive_news/4214386.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">Automotive X-Prize</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"> of $25 Million (or more) is successfully claimed by a car designed to meet the 100 mpg/mass-producable goal set by the </span><A href="http://autoxprize.org/"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">X Prize Foundation</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">.&nbsp; Numerous companies spring forth out of prototypes tested in the contest.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2010</strong> : </span><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/12/the_tesla_roads.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">Tesla Motors releases a fully electric 4-door family sedan for $50,000 to $70,000</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">.&nbsp; With gasoline at $4/gallon, established automobile companies simultaneously release </span><A href="http://www.popularmechanics.com/automotive/new_cars/4215489.html?series=19"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">plug-in hybrid vehicles</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">.&nbsp; <A href="http://www.techreview.com/Energy/18247/">Hybrid, plug-in hybrid, and fully electrical cars represent 5% of total new automobiles sold in the US</A>, even if tax incentives have been a large stimulus.&nbsp; The habit of plugging in a car overnight to charge it starts to become routine for homeowners with such cars, but apartment dwellers are at a disadvantage in this regard, not having an outlet near their parking spot.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2011 :</strong> Two or more iPod ports, 10-inch flat-screen displays for back seat passengers, parking space detection technology, and embedded Wi-Fi adapters that wirelessly can transfer files into the vehicle's hard drive from up to 500 feet away are standard features for many new cars in the $40,000+ price tier.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2012 </strong>: Over 100 million new automobiles are produced in 2012, up from 70 million in 2006.&nbsp; All major auto manufacturers are racing to incorporate new nanomaterials that are lighter than aluminium yet stronger and more malleable than steel.&nbsp; The average weight of cars has dropped by about 5% from what it was for the equivalent style in 2007.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2013</strong> : </span><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/12/the_tesla_roads.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">Tesla Motors releases a fully electric</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"> 4-door sedan that is available for under $40,000, which is only 33% more than the $30,000 that the typical fully-loaded gasoline-only V6 Accord or Camry sells for in 2013.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2014 :</strong> Self-driving cars are now available in the luxury tier (priced $100,000 or higher).&nbsp; A user simply enters in the destination, and the car charts out a path (similar to Google Maps) and proceeds on it, in compliance with traffic laws.&nbsp; However, a software malfunction results in a major traffic pile-up that garners national media attention for a week.&nbsp; Subsequently, self-driving technologies are shunned despite their superior statistical performance relative to human drivers.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2015</strong> : </span><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/02/why_70barrel_oi.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">As predicted in early 2006 on The Futurist</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">, a 4-door sedan with a 240 hp engine, yet costing only 5 cents/mile to operate (the equivalent of 60 mpg of gasoline), is widely available for $35,000 (which is within the middle-class price band by 2015 <A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2007/07/economic-growth.html">under </A></span><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2007/07/economic-growth.html">moderate assumptions for economic growth</A></span><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">).&nbsp; This is the result of combined advances in energy, lighter nanomaterials, and computerized systems.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2016</strong> : An odd change has occurred in the economics of car depreciation.&nbsp; Between 1980 and 2007, annual car depreciation rates decreased due to higher quality materials and better engine design, reaching as little as 12-16% a year for the first 5 years of ownership.&nbsp; Technology pushed back the forces of depreciation.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">However, by 2016, 40% of a car's initial purchase price is comprised of electronics (up from under 20% in 2007 and just 5% in 1985), which depreciate at a rate of 25-40% a year.&nbsp; The entire value of the car is pulled along by the 40% of it that undergoes rapid price declines, and thus total car depreciation is now occuring at a faster rate of up to 20% a year for the first 5 years.&nbsp; This is a natural progression of <A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/02/the_impact_of_c.html">The Impact of Computing</A>, and wealthier consumers are increasingly buying new cars as 'upgrades' to replace models with obsolete technologies after 5-7 years, much as they would upgrade a game console, rather than waiting until mechanical failure occurs in their current car.&nbsp; Consumers also conduct their own upgrades of certain easily-replaced components, much as they would upgrade the memory or hard drive of a PC. Technology has thus accelerated the forces of depreciation.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2018</strong> : Among new cars sold, gasoline-only vehicles are now a minority.&nbsp; Millions of electricity-only vehicles are charged through solar panels on a daily basis, relieving those consumers of a fuel expenditure that was as high as $2000/year in 2007.&nbsp; Even when sunlight is obscured and the grid is used, some electrical vehicles cost as little as 1 cent/mile to operate. </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2020 :</strong> New safety technologies that began to appear in mainstream cars around 2012, <A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/03/advances_in_aut.html">such as night vision, lane departure correction, and collision-avoiding cruise control</A>, have replaced the existing fleet of older cars over the decade, and now US annual traffic fatalities have dropped to 25,000 in 2020 from 43,000 in 2005.&nbsp; Given the larger US population in 2020 (about 350 Million), this is a reduction in traffic deaths by half on a per-capita basis.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2024 :</strong> Self-driving cars have overcome the stigma of a decade prior, and are now widely used.&nbsp; But they still have not fully displaced manual driving, due to user preferences in this regard.&nbsp; Certain highways permit only self-driven cars, with common speed limits of 100 mph or more.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em"><strong>2025-30</strong> : Electricity (indeed, <em>clean</em> electricity) now fuels nearly all passenger car miles driven in the US.&nbsp; There is no longer any significant fuel consumption cost associated with driving a car, although battery maintenance is a new aspect of car ownership.&nbsp; Many car bodies now include solar energy absorbant materials that charge a parked car during periods of sunlight.&nbsp; Leaving such cars out in the sun has supplanted the practice of parking in the shade or in covered parking. </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">Pervasive use of advanced nanomaterials has ensured that the average car weighs only 60% as much as a 2008 counterpart, but yet is over twice as resistant to dents.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">______________________________________________________________</span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 1.2em">I believe that this timeline represents the the combination of median forecasts across all technological and economic trends that influence cars, and will be perceived as too optimistic or too pessimistic by an equal number of readers.&nbsp; Let's see how closely reality matches this timeline.&nbsp; </span></P>
-----

TITLE: Surfaces : The Next Killer Ap in Computing
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: surface-interfaces-the-next-killer-ap-in-computing
CATEGORY: Accelerating Change
CATEGORY: Computing
CATEGORY: Consumer Technology
CATEGORY: Internet
CATEGORY: Technological Breakthroughs
CATEGORY: The Singularity

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/07/surface-interfaces-the-next-killer-ap-in-computing.html 
DATE: 07/11/2008 06:22:39 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><A style="DISPLAY: block" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e200e5539742bb8833-pi"><font color=#000000></font></A></span><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Computing, once seamlessly synonymous with technological progress, has not grabbed headlines in recent memory. We have not had a 'killer ap' in computing in the last few years.&nbsp; Maybe you can count Wi-fi access to laptops in 2002-03 as the most recent one, but if that is not a sufficiently&nbsp;important innovation, we then have to go all the way back to the graphical World Wide Web browser in 1995.&nbsp; Before that, the killer ap was Microsoft Office for Windows in 1990.&nbsp; Clearly, such shifts appear to&nbsp;occur at&nbsp;intervals of 5-8 years.&nbsp; </P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">I can, without hesitation, nominate surface computing as the next great generational augmentation in the computing experience.&nbsp; This is because surface computing entirely transforms the human-computer interaction in a matter that is more suitable for the human body than the mouse/keyboard model is.&nbsp; In accordance with the </span></span></span><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/02/the_impact_of_c.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Impact of Computing</span></span></span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">, rapid drops in the costs of both high-definition displays and tactile sensors are set to bring this experience to consumers by the end of this decade.&nbsp; </span></span></span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><A style="DISPLAY: block" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e200e553b2825e8834-pi"><img  class="at-xid-6a00d83452455969e200e553b2825e8834 image-full " title=Surface style="WIDTH: 543px; HEIGHT: 289px" height=322 alt=Surface src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e200e553b2825e8834-800wi" width=573 border=0></A> </span></span></span></P>
<P><A href="http://images.businessweek.com/ss/08/07/0701_surface_computing/index.htm"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">BusinessWeek has a slideshow featuring several different products for surface computing.</span></span></span></span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">&nbsp; Over ten major electronics companies have surface computing products available.&nbsp; The most visible is the </span></span></span></span><A href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft_surface"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Microsoft Surface</span></span></span></span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">, which sells for about $10,000, but will probably drop to $3000 or less within 3-4 years, enabling&nbsp;household adoption.&nbsp; </span></span></span></span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">As far as early applications of surface computing, a fertile imagination can yield many prospects.&nbsp; For example, a restaurant table may feature a surface that displays the menu, enabling patrons to order simply by touching the picture of the item they choose.&nbsp; The information is sent to the kitchen, and this saves time and reduces the number of waiters needed by the restaurant (as waiters would only be needed to deliver the completed orders).&nbsp; Applications&nbsp;for classroom and video game settings also readily present themselves.&nbsp; </span></span></span></span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Watch for demonstrations of various surface computers at your local electronics store, and keep an eye on the price drops.&nbsp;&nbsp;After seeing a demonstration,&nbsp;do share&nbsp;at what pricepoint you might purchase one.&nbsp; The next generation of computing beckons.</span>&nbsp; </span></span></span></P>
<P style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Related :</span></span></span></P>
<P><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2006/02/the_impact_of_c.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">The Impact of Computing</A></P></span></span></span></span>

TITLE: More on the Upcoming Internet Gridlock.

DATE: 06/26/2008 04:12:34 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><A href="http://www.technologyreview.com/Infotech/20919/?a=f"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">MIT Technology Review has a 6-page article detailing the imminent choking of the Internet under increasing bandwidth volume</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">.&nbsp; This follows </span><A href="http://www.techsectorweb.com/2008/03/internet-traffi.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">my post from March 13, 2008 about the continued rapid growth in Internet traffic due to video applications</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">While everyone worries about the daily rise in oil prices (</span><A href="http://www.singularity2050.com/2007/10/why-i-want-oil-.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">which I contend has many unnoticed positive side effects</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">), there is far too little attention given to the upcoming Internet slowdown.&nbsp; It has just as much potential to hinder commerce as higher energy prices, and a fast, uncongested Internet even has a direct role to play in cushioning the impact of higher energy prices through enabling the advance of high-quality video conferencing technologies.&nbsp; Read much more about the relation between oil prices and Internet bandwith demand in my article </span><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/08/terrorism_oil_b.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Terrorism, Oil, Globalization, and the Impact of Computing</span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">.&nbsp; </span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Of course, just as anyone who bet on the rise in oil prices could have profited by taking a position in any number of exchange-traded energy securities, similar opportunities for profit abound from the upcoming bandwidth scarcity.&nbsp; Cisco Systems (CSCO) would be the most obvious beneficiary, but a number of smaller companies also present opportunities.&nbsp; More analysis on these will follow in coming weeks.</span>&nbsp; </P>

TITLE: The Solar Revolution is Near
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1

DATE: 05/20/2008 01:20:08 PM
-----
BODY:
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">I have long been optimistic about Solar Energy (whether photovoltaic or thermal) becoming our largest energy source within a couple of&nbsp;decades.&nbsp; Earlier articles on the subject include :</P>
<P><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2007/02/a_future_timeli.html">A Future Timeline for Energy</A></P>
<P><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2007/08/solar-energy-co.html">Solar Energy Cost Curve</A></P>
<P>Several recent events and developments have led me to reinforce this view.&nbsp; First of all, <A href="http://www.sciam.com/article.cfm?id=a-solar-grand-plan"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">consider this article from Scientific American, detailing a Solar timeline to 2050.</span></A> The article is not even Singularity-aware, yet details many steps that will enable Solar energy to expand by orders of magnitude above the level that it is today.&nbsp; Secondly, two of the most uniquely brilliant people alive today, Ray Kurzweil and Elon Musk (who I recently was fortuante enough to chat with), have both provided compelling cases on why Solar will be our largest energy source by 2030.&nbsp; Both Kurzweil and Musk reside in significantly different spheres, yet have arrived at the same prediction. </P>
<P></P>
<P>However, the third point is the one that I find to be the most compelling. There are a number of publicly traded companies selling solar energy products, many of which had IPOs in just the last three years.&nbsp; Some of these companies, and their market capitalizations,&nbsp;are : </P>
<P><A style="DISPLAY: inline" onclick="window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=296,height=140,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e200e5524927a68833-popup"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><img  class="at-xid-6a00d83452455969e200e5524927a68833 " alt=Solar1 src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e200e5524927a68833-500pi"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"></span></P>
<P style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">Now consider that the companies on this list alone amount to about $50 Billion in capitalization.&nbsp; There are, additionally, many smaller companies not included on this list, many companies like Applied Materials (AMAT) and Cypress Semiconductor (CY) for which solar&nbsp;products comprise only a portion of their business, and large private companies like NanoSolar (</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><A href="http://http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2008/02/nine-tantalizin.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">which I have heavily profiled here</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px">) and SolFocus that may have valuations in the billions.&nbsp; Thus, the market cap of the 'solar sector' is already between $60B and $100B, depending on what you include within the total.&nbsp; This immense valuation has accumulated at a pace that has&nbsp;taken many casual observers by surprise.&nbsp; A 2-year chart of some of the stocks listed above tells the story (click to enlarge) :.&nbsp; </span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></P>
<P><A style="DISPLAY: block" onclick="window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=808,height=343,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e200e55260fb2e8834-popup"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><A style="DISPLAY: inline" onclick="window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=808,height=343,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e200e552492dc68833-popup"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></A><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"></span><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"></span><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"></span><A style="DISPLAY: block" onclick="window.open( this.href, '_blank', 'width=808,height=343,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0' ); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e200e5524956f98833-popup"><img  class="at-xid-6a00d83452455969e200e5524956f98833 image-full " title=Solar2 height=331 alt=Solar2 src="https://futurist.typepad.com/.a/6a00d83452455969e200e5524956f98833-800pi" width=779 border=0></A> </P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px; FONT-FAMILY: Arial">While FirstSolar (FSLR) has been the brightest star, all the others have trounced the S&amp;P500 to a degree that would put even Google or Apple to shame over this period.&nbsp; Clearly, a dramatic&nbsp;ramp in Solar energy is about to make mainstream headlines very soon, even if the present valuations are too high.&nbsp; </span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></P>
<P><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px; FONT-FAMILY: Arial"><strong>Is this a dot-com-like bubble?</strong>&nbsp; Yes, in the near-term, it is.&nbsp; However, after a sharp correction, the long term growth will resume for the companies that emerge as leaders.&nbsp; I won't recommend a specific stock among this cluster just yet, as there are a wave of private companies with new technologies that could render any of these incumbents obsolete.&nbsp; Specific company profiles will follow soon, but in the meantime, for more detail on the long-term trends in favor of Solar,&nbsp;refer to&nbsp;these additional articles of mine :</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></P>
<P><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2007/10/why-i-want-oil-.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px; FONT-FAMILY: Arial">Why I Want Oil to Hit $120 per Barrel</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></A></P>
<P><A href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/08/terrorism_oil_b.html"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px"><span style="FONT-SIZE: 14px; FONT-FAMILY: Arial">Terrorism, Oil, Globalization, and the Impact of Computing</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></A></P></span></span>

AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: The Activision Pick Delivers Yet Again
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: the-activision
CATEGORY: Consumer Technology
CATEGORY: Stock Picks

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/05/the-activision.html 
DATE: 05/10/2008 03:06:51 PM
-----
BODY:
<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">For those of you who have been reading my articles on the video game sector on The Futurist, know that I have been a fan of Activision (ATVI) since 2006.&nbsp; It was first identified, <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/04/the_next_big_th_2.html">along with other gaming companies, on April 17, 2006</a>.&nbsp; After that, <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2008/01/the-futurists-s.html">ATVI was a component of my 2007 portfolio, earning a 69.2% return</a>.&nbsp; Even for 2008, while I shed the other gaming stocks like ERTS, THQI, and retailer GME, <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2007/11/the-futurists-s.html">I kept Activision as the only gaming stock, and one of only three tech stocks, in my 2008 portfolio</a>.&nbsp; Yet, it has delivered a 50% return since the start of the portfolio, after its strong Q1 results last Thursday.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=800,height=311,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/05/10/atvi_2.jpg"></a><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=800,height=311,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/05/10/atvi_3.jpg"><img title="Atvi_3" height="248" alt="Atvi_3" src="http://www.techsectorweb.com/images/2008/05/10/atvi_3.jpg" width="640" border="0" /></a>&nbsp; </p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">Everyone knows that the 'Guitar Hero' game is a blockbuster, but what is less understood is how such games that have customized controllers (a guitar, in this case) are the future of gaming.&nbsp; The multi-button controller is not appealing to adults at all, and as the guitar controller eventually inspires skis, boxing gloves, golf clubs, etc., the realism and hence market appeal of games broadens to even more demographics.&nbsp; Just note the success of the Nintendo Wii as another indicator of this trend.&nbsp; Read my article <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/04/the_next_big_th.html">&quot;The Next Big Thing in Entertainment&quot;</a> on The Futurist for an in-depth analysis of this subject.&nbsp; Activision is set to launch three new Guiter Hero games in 2008, placing the company at the forefront of this major trend.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">The stock might be nearing the end of a momentum run after such a spectacular return, and I may cease to include it in future portfolios until it pulls back to a better entry point, but as a long-term bet in the technology sector, Activision is still one of the best stocks to hold.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">Related :</span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;"><a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/04/the_next_big_th.html">The Next Big Thing in Entertainment</a></span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;"><a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/01/the_technologic.html">The Technological Progression of Video Games</a></span></p>

TITLE: Energy vs. Financials, A Divergence of Historical Extremes
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: energy-vs-finan
CATEGORY: Economics
CATEGORY: Stock Market Inflection Points
CATEGORY: Stock Picks

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/04/energy-vs-finan.html 
DATE: 04/22/2008 11:52:43 AM
-----
BODY:
<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">Energy and Financials are both large sectoral components of the S&amp;P500.&nbsp; Yet the two have diverged immensely over the last 2 years.&nbsp; Not since the technology bust at the start of the decade have any two sectors diverged so much from each other, and from the composite S&amp;P500 index.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">XLE is an exchanged-traded fund for the Energy sector, while XLF is the equivalent for the Financial sector.&nbsp; First, let us view a two-year chart :&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;"><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=800,height=338,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/04/22/xlexlf2.jpg"></a><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=800,height=338,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/04/22/xlexlf2_2.jpg"><img title="Xlexlf2_2" height="287" alt="Xlexlf2_2" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/techsector/images/2008/04/22/xlexlf2_2.jpg" width="608" border="0" style="FLOAT: left; MARGIN: 0px 5px 5px 0px; WIDTH: 608px; HEIGHT: 287px" /></a></span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">Energy has outperformed the S&amp;P500 by an equal margin that Financials have lagged the S&amp;P500 by.&nbsp; Next, we can view a five-year chart :</span></p>

<p><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=800,height=327,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/04/22/xlfxle_2.jpg"><img title="Xlfxle_2" height="277" alt="Xlfxle_2" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/techsector/images/2008/04/22/xlfxle_2.jpg" width="608" border="0" style="FLOAT: left; MARGIN: 0px 5px 5px 0px; WIDTH: 608px; HEIGHT: 277px" /></a></p>

<p><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=800,height=327,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/04/22/xlfxle.jpg"></a></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">While Financials only began to fall away in 2007, Energy has gone so high above the composite market that it reminds one of the technology bubble of the late 1990s.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">It seems quite obvious here that while it is impossible to identify the exact top of the Energy run, or the exact bottom of the Financials correction, it would be very prudent to sell any existing holdings in Energy (or even short Energy if you have the appetite) and rotate the proceeds into Financials.&nbsp; The gap could widen in the short term, but rarely do two sectors reach such extreme disparities that make a profitable trade so obvious.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">(<a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2008/04/energy-vs-finan.html">crossposted on The Futurist</a>).</span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;"><a href="http://businesscycle.com/news/press/1476/">The Economic Cycle Research Institute has declared the US economy to be in recession</a>.&nbsp; This is <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/techsector/2008/02/us-recession-be.html">six weeks after I declared the same on TechSector</a>, and pinpointed the start of the recession to be December 2007.&nbsp; &nbsp;</span></p>

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/03/internet-traffi.html

-----
BODY:
<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;"><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=508,height=655,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/03/13/internet_traffic_2.jpg"></a><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=508,height=655,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/03/13/internet_traffic_3.jpg"><img title="Internet_traffic_3" height="589" alt="Internet_traffic_3" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/techsector/images/2008/03/13/internet_traffic_3.jpg" width="457" border="0" style="FLOAT: right; MARGIN: 0px 0px 5px 5px" /></a>The New York Times published a chart from Cisco Systems that projects estimated Internet traffic growth through 2010.&nbsp; Of course, Cisco has the most to gain from showing very high growth in traffic, but this is still a slower rate of growth than was seen in the 1990s, indicating some maturity in terms of new Internet users worldwide.</span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">This confirms <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/techsector/2008/01/world-broadband.html">my proprietary forecasts on world Broadband subscribers and average household Broadband speeds</a> very nicely.&nbsp; The growth in household Broadband speeds may still not be fast enough to swallow the increase in terabytes of traffic between now and 2010.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">All this is good news not just for networking companies, but for content providers, PC makers, cellphone makers, search engines, etc.&nbsp; Keep in mind that Internet traffic growth is driven not just by new Internet users (only 1 billion out of 6.5 billion people have used the Internet to date), but also the fact that video files can be produced, duplicated, and distributed infinitely.</span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">&nbsp; </span></p>

AUTHOR: The Futurist
AUTHOR EMAIL: kartik_gada@yahoo.com
TITLE: Is Technology Diffusion in a Lull?
STATUS: Draft
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: is-technology-d
CATEGORY: Accelerating Change
CATEGORY: Computing
CATEGORY: Technological Breakthroughs
CATEGORY: The Singularity

UNIQUE URL: https://atom.singularity2050.com/2008/02/is-technology-d.html 
DATE: 02/29/2008 06:04:40 PM
-----
BODY:
<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">There are minor but growing elements of evidence that the rate of technological change has moderated in this decade.&nbsp; Whether this is a temporary trough that merely precedes a return to the trendline, or whether the trendline itself was greatly overestimated, will not be decisively known for some years.&nbsp; In this article, I will attempt to examine some datapoints to determine whether we are at, or behind, where we would expect to be in 2008.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">There is overwhelming evidence that <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/12/are_you_acceler.html">many seemingly unrelated technologies are progressing at an accelerating rate</a>.&nbsp; However, the exact magnitude to the accelerating gradient - the second derivative - is difficult to measure with precision.&nbsp; Furthermore, there are periods where advancement can be significantly above or below any such trendline.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">This brings us to the chart below from Ray Kurzweil (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Image:PPTMASSuseInventionsLogPRINT.jpg">from Wikipedia</a>) :</span></p>

<p><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=752,height=600,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/02/18/752pxpptmassuseinventionslogprint.jpg"></a><span style="font-size: 1.2em;"><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=752,height=600,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/02/19/752pxpptmassuseinventionslogprint.jpg"></a><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=752,height=600,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/02/19/752pxpptmassuseinventionslogprint_2.jpg"></a><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=752,height=600,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/02/29/752pxpptmassuseinventionslogprint.jpg"></a><a onclick="window.open(this.href, '_blank', 'width=752,height=600,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0'); return false" href="http://futurist.typepad.com/.shared/image.html?/photos/uncategorized/2008/02/29/752pxpptmassuseinventionslogprint_2.jpg"><img title="752pxpptmassuseinventionslogprint_2" height="480" alt="752pxpptmassuseinventionslogprint_2" src="https://futurist.typepad.com/techsector/images/2008/02/29/752pxpptmassuseinventionslogprint_2.jpg" width="601" border="0" style="FLOAT: left; MARGIN: 0px 5px 5px 0px" /></a>&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">This chart appears prominently in many of Kurzweil's writings, and brilliantly conveys the concept of how each major consumer technology reached the mainstream (as defined by a 25% US household penetration rate) in successively shorter times.&nbsp; The horizontal axis represents the year in which the technology was invented.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">This chart was produced some years ago, and therein lies the problem.&nbsp; If we were to update the chart to the present day, which technology would be the next addition after 'The Web'?&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">Many technologies can claim to be the ones to occupy the next position on the chart.&nbsp; IPods and other portable mp3 players, various Web 2.0 applications like social networking, and flat-panel TVs all reached the 25% level of mainstream adoption in under 6 years in accordance with an extrapolation of the chart through 2008.&nbsp; However, it is debatable that any of these are 'revolutionary' technologies like the ones on the chart, rather than merely increments above incumbent predecessors.&nbsp; The iPod merely improved upon the capacity and flexibility of the walkman, the plasma TV merely consumed less space than the tube TV, etc.&nbsp; The technologies on the chart are all <em>infrastructures</em> of some sort, and it is clear that after 'The Web', we are challenged to find a suitable candidate for the next entry.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">Thus, we either are on the brink of some overdue technology emerging to reach 25% penetration of US households in 6 years or less, or the rapid diffusion of the Internet truly was a historical anomaly, and for the period from 2001 to 2008 we were merely correcting back to a trendline of much slower diffusion (where it take 10-15 years for a technology to each 25% penetration in the US).&nbsp; One of the two has to be true, at least for an affluent society like the US.</span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">This brings us to the third and final dimension of possibility.&nbsp; This being the decade of globalization, <a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/12/what_determines.html">with globalization itself being an expected natural progression of technological change</a>, perhaps a US-centric chart itself was inappropriate to begin with.&nbsp; Landline telephones and television sets still do not have 25% penetration in countries like India, but mobile phones jumped from zero to 10% penetration in under 7 years.&nbsp; The oft-cited 'leapfrogging' of technologies that developing nations can benefit from is a crucial piece of technological diffusion, which would thus show a much smaller interval between 'telephones' and 'mobile phones' than in the US-based chart above.&nbsp; Perhaps '10% Worldwide Household Penetration' is a more suitable measure than '25% US Household Penetration', which would then possibly show that there is no lull in worldwide technological adoption at all.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">I may try to put together this new worldwide chart.&nbsp; The horizontal axis would not change, but the placement of datapoints along the vertical axis would.&nbsp; Perhaps Kurzweil merely has to break out of US-centricity in order to strengthen his case and rebut most of his critics.&nbsp; </span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">The future will disclose the results to us soon enough.</span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">(<a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2008/02/is-technology-d.html">crossposted on The Futurist</a>)</span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;">Related :</span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;"><a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/12/are_you_acceler.html">Are You Acceleration Aware?</a></span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;"><a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/02/the_impact_of_c.html">The Impact of Computing</a></span></p>

<p><span style="font-size: 1.2em;"><a href="http://futurist.typepad.com/my_weblog/2006/03/these_are_the_b.html">These are the Best of Times</a></span></p>
